Loss of Books: A Cause of Hadith Variation
Explains how loss of written records leads to reliance on memory, introducing errors in narration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how loss of a narrator's book affects the reliability of his narrations and contributes to ikhtilaf among fuqaha.

Core Idea
Loss of a narrator's book shifts reliance from precise records to fallible memory, increasing error risk and causing variations.

In the previous causes of ikhtilaf, we learned that precision (dabt) has two forms: memory-precision (dabt al-sadr) and book-precision (dabt al-kitab). A narrator who depends primarily on his book is considered reliable as long as his book is preserved. But when the book is lost and he narrates from memory, the probability of error rises. This lesson examines how such loss creates textual variations that later fuel fiqh differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of precision
Recall that dabt al-sadr (memory precision) and dabt al-kitab (book precision) are the two categories. A narrator may rely on either, but the standard of reliability differs.
Book precision means the narrator's notes are accurate and he guards them; memory precision means he accurately memorises and recalls.
2
Understand the shift from book to memory
When a narrator loses his book, he can no longer rely on his written record. If he continues narrating, he must depend on what he remembers. Since his original authority was the book, memory-based narration is more prone to errors and illusions (wahm).
The author states: 'since their original reliance in narration was on their books, not on their memory, the occurrence of error and illusion in those narrations is possible.'
3
Trace the causal chain to variation
Errors and illusions in hadith texts produce variant wordings, additions, or omissions. These textual differences are one source of ikhtilaf among scholars who rely on those narrations for legal rulings.
The chain: loss of book → memory narration → error → variant hadith → different fiqh conclusions.
4
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
In Hanafi usul, a narrator whose book is lost and who narrates from memory is considered less reliable unless his memory is independently verified. This affects acceptance of his narrations in istidlal (legal deduction) and may lead to preferring other narrations.
Hanafi scholars require dabit (precise) narrators; loss of book undermines dabt.

Source Text

خامساً. ذهاب الكتب:
قد علمنا مما سبق أن ضبط الكتاب هو أحد قسمي الضبط، والعمدة في هذا القسم على كتاب الراوي وتطرق الخلل إلى كتابه أمر مضر بالثقة في مرويات ذلك الراوي، وقد يصل الأمر إلى أن يدع الراوي روايته جملة بسبب فقد كتابه. إلا أن بعض الرواة قد يعلق في أذهانهم شيء من تلك المرويات التي دونوها في كتبهم المفقودة، فيحدثون بها، ولما كان معتمدهم أصلاً في الرواية على كتبهم لا على حفظهم فإن وجود الخطأ والوهم في تلك الروايات وارد. ومن رواة الأحاديث الذين ذهبت كتبهم مع اعتمادهم على تلك الكتب في
Fifth: Loss of books. We have learned from the foregoing that book-precision is one of the two types of precision. The reliance in this type is on the narrator's book, and impairment of his book is harmful to trust in that narrator's narrations. It may reach the point that the narrator abandons his narration altogether due to the loss of his book. However, some narrators may retain in their minds something of those narrations they had recorded in their lost books, and they narrate them. Since their original reliance in narration was on their books, not on their memory, the occurrence of error and illusion in those narrations is possible. Among the hadith narrators whose books were lost while they relied on those books for...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth cause of variation: the loss of written records. The author reminds us that book-precision (dabt al-kitab) is one of the two types of precision. If a narrator's book is lost or impaired, it undermines trust in his narrations, and he might even stop narrating. However, some narrators continue to narrate from memory what they had previously recorded. Since their original authority was their written record, not their memory, errors and illusions are likely to creep into such narrations. This leads to differences among hadith texts. The Hanafi school, like others, requires reliability in transmission; loss of books affects that reliability and hence contributes to fiqh differences.

Key Terms
ذهاب الكتب (dhahab al-kutub): Loss of written records containing hadith narrations
ضبط الكتاب (dabt al-kitab): Precision in maintaining and relying on one's written records

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The narrator's narrations become absolutely reliable because he now depends on his strong memory.
B) The narrator's narrations are automatically rejected as unreliable.
C) The possibility of error and illusion in those narrations arises.
D) The narrator loses his trustworthiness entirely.

Key Takeaway
Loss of a narrator's written records forces reliance on memory, which increases the probability of error and thereby contributes to hadith variations and later fiqh differences.

Blindness as a Cause of Disagreement in Hadith Narration
Explains how loss of eyesight leads to errors in hadith narration and provides historical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a narrator's loss of eyesight can introduce errors (wahm) into hadith narrations and identify two historical examples of narrators affected by blindness.

Core Idea
Blindness forces reliance on memory, increasing the risk of wahm and causing disagreement among narrations.

The accuracy of hadith transmission depends on both memory and visual verification. When a narrator loses his sight, he must rely solely on memory, which can falter. This lesson examines how historical narrators like Abd al-Razzaq and Ali ibn Musahhir suffered changes after losing their sight, illustrating a cause of ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
Recognize that the passage identifies loss of eyesight as a factor that can introduce errors (wahm) into hadith narration.
2
Explain the mechanism
Understand that sight aids in preserving memory; without it, narrators rely on memory alone, which can be fallible, leading to discrepancies.
3
Examine the evidence
Analyze the two examples given: Abd al-Razzaq al-San'ani and Ali ibn Musahhir. Note what was said about each after they lost their sight.
4
Connect to ikhtilaf
Understand how such errors cause disagreements between narrations, which in turn affect juristic rulings that rely on those hadiths.
5
Evaluate the impact on narrator grading
Consider that blindness does not automatically make a narrator unreliable; it increases the probability of error, but some blind narrators remain trustworthy with external verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمفردهم، وقسم مِنْهُمْ يستعين بمن يثق بِهِ ليعاونه عَلَى ذَلِكَ. إذن فالبصر مهم في ذَلِكَ وله دور كبير في المحافظة على الحفظ؛ لذا فإنّ زوال البصر وذهابه قد يؤدي بالمحصلة النهائية إلى دخول الوهم في بعض روايات الْمُحَدِّثِينَ مِمَّا يؤدي إلى حصول اختلاف بين الروايات.
ومن الَّذِينَ ذهب بصرهم: عبد الرزاق بن همام الصنعاني صاحب المصنف قال الحافظ ابن حجر العسقلاني: عمي في آخر عمره فتغير. وكذا علي بن مسهر قَالَ العجلي: «صاحب سنة ثقة في الْحَدِيث صالح الكتاب كثير الرِوَايَة عَنْ الكوفيين»، وَقَالَ أبو عَبْد الله أحمد بن حَنْبَل لما سئل عَنْهُ: «لا أدري كيف أقول كَانَ قد ذهب بصره فكان يحدثهم من حفظه).
By themselves, and some of them seek help from someone they trust to assist them in that. So sight is important in that and has a great role in preserving memory; hence, the loss of sight may ultimately lead to the entry of illusion in some narrations of the hadith scholars, leading to disagreement between narrations. Among those who lost their sight: Abd al-Razzaq ibn Hammam al-San'ani, author of al-Musannaf; al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani said: He went blind at the end of his life and changed. Likewise, Ali ibn Musahhir; al-Ijli said: (He was) a person of Sunnah, trustworthy in hadith, righteous in writing, many narrations from the Kufans. And Abu Abd Allah Ahmad ibn Hanbal, when asked about him, said: I don't know how to say; he had lost his sight and would narrate to them from memory.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is from the introductory chapter on the nature of disagreement. It identifies the loss of eyesight as a key factor that can lead to errors in hadith narration, thus causing disagreement among narrations. The author first explains that sight is crucial for preserving hadith, since narrators often memorize orally and rely on visual cues. When a narrator goes blind, he may rely on memory alone, and memory can falter. The author provides examples: Abd al-Razzaq al-San'ani, who went blind in old age and changed, and Ali ibn Musahhir, who was trustworthy but after losing his sight narrated from memory, which raised concern for Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal. This illustrates that even reliable narrators can become prone to error due to blindness, leading to discrepancies in their narrations.

Key Terms
عمى (ama): Blindness; loss of eyesight affecting a narrator's ability to verify his narrations.
وهم (wahm): Error or illusion in a hadith narration that can arise from reliance on memory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He went blind in his later years and changed.
C) He narrated only from memory.
D) He was a weak narrator.

Key Takeaway
Loss of eyesight can introduce wahm in narrations, but does not automatically disqualify a narrator; it increases the risk of error.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Loss of Books as a Cause of Disagreement in Hadith Narrations
Explaining how loss of books leads to errors in narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the loss or absence of a narrator's written records affects the reliability of hadith narrations and identify the key concept of dabt al-kitab.

Core Idea
A narrator's written record is the basis of reliability; its loss or absence undermines their narrations.

In hadith criticism, a narrator's accuracy (dabt) can be based on memory or written records. Some narrators rely heavily on their books. When those books are lost or absent, errors and illusions become likely if they narrate from memory. This is a recognized cause of disagreement among scholars regarding the authenticity of certain narrations. In this lesson, we examine this principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dabt
Recognize that dabt is divided into dabt al-sadr (memorization) and dabt al-kitab (written record). The passage states that dabt al-kitab is one of the two types.
This distinction is foundational in Hanafi usul for evaluating ahad narrations.
2
Understand the impact of losing books
When a narrator relies on his book for accuracy, loss of that book can lead to errors if he continues narrating from memory. The passage uses the word 'يعتمد' (relies) to emphasize dependency.
3
Analyze the example of Ibn Lahi'a
Ibn Lahi'a was precise in his book, but after his books burned, his narrations became weak because his reliance was on writing, not memory. This illustrates the principle.
The text quotes Imam Ahmad and others to establish his initial reliability.
4
Recognize the effect of temporary absence
The passage extends the principle: even temporary absence of books (e.g., while traveling) has the same effect as permanent loss, leading to potential errors.
The phrase 'ربما يكون لغياب الكتب نفس أثر فقد الكتب' explicitly states this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامساً. ذهاب الكتب: قد علمنا مِمَّا سبق أن ضبط الكتاب هُوَ أحد قسمي الضبط، والعمدة في هَذَا القسم على كتاب الرَّاوِي وتطرق الخلل إلى كتابه أمر مضر بالثقة في مرويات ذلِكَ الرَّاوِي، وَقَدْ يصل الأمر إلى أن يدع الرَّاوِي روايته جملة بسبب فقد كتابه. إلا أن بعض الرُّوَاة قَدْ يعلق في أذهانهم شيء من تلك المرويات التي دونوها في كتبهم المفقودة، فيحدّثون بها، ولما كَانَ معتمدهم أصلاً في الرِّوَايَة عَلَى كتبهم لا عَلَى حفظهم فإن وجود الخطأ والوهم في تلك الروايات وارد. ومن رواة الأحاديث الَّذِيْنَ ذهبت كتبهم مع اعتمادهم عَلَى تِلْكَ الكتب في حفظهم عَبْد الله بن لهيعة، أبو عَبْد الرَّحْمن الحضرمي، الفقيه قاضي مصر، كَانَ متقناً لكتابه، قال الإمام أحمد : ابن لهيعة أجود قراءة لكتبه من ابن وهب. وَقَدْ كَانَ جل اعتماده في روايته عَلَى كتبه، فلما احترقت ضُعَف في الرِّوَايَة لكثرة ما وجد من الوهم والخطأ في روايته بَعْدَ ذهاب كتبه. قَالَ إسحاق بن عيسى الطباع: احترقت كتب ابن لهيعة سنة تسع وستين. وَقَالَ البخاري عَنْ يحيى بن بكير: احترق منزل ابن لهيعة وكتبه في سنة سبعين ومئة.
وربما يَكُون لغياب الكتب نَفْسُ أثرِ فَقْدِ الكتب ويكون مدعاة للوهم والخلاف، فإذا حدّث الرَّاوِي - الَّذِي يعتمد في الأداء عَلَى كتابه - في حالة غياب كتبه عنه، وقع الوهم والخطأ في حديثه، وتحديثه في غير بلده - أيضاً - مظنة لوقوع ذَلِكَ كَمَا حصل
Fifthly: Loss of books. We have learned from what preceded that the accuracy of the book is one of the two types of accuracy, and reliance in this type is on the book of the narrator, and defect entering his book is harmful to confidence in the narrations of that narrator. It may reach the point that the narrator abandons his narrations entirely due to the loss of his book. However, some narrators may retain in their minds something of those narrations which they had written in their lost books, and they narrate them. Since their original reliance in narration was on their books, not on their memory, then the presence of error and illusion in those narrations is likely. Among the hadith narrators whose books were lost while they relied on those books in their memory is Abd Allah ibn Lahi'a, Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Hadrami, the jurist and judge of Egypt. He was precise in his book. Imam Ahmad said: Ibn Lahi'a read his books better than Ibn Wahb. And his reliance in his narration was on his books. So when they burned, he became weak in narration due to the many errors and mistakes found in his narrations after the loss of his books. Ishaq ibn Isa al-Tabba' said: Ibn Lahi'a's books burned in the year sixty-nine. And al-Bukhari reported from Yahya ibn Bukayr: The house of Ibn Lahi'a and his books burned in the year seventy and one hundred. And perhaps the absence of books has the same effect as the loss of books and becomes a cause for illusion and disagreement. So if the narrator who relies in performance on his book narrates while his books are absent, error and mistake occur in his hadith. Also, narrating outside his town is a place where that may occur, as happened.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of factors causing disagreement, now focusing on the loss of books. The author states that when a narrator relies on his book for accuracy, the loss of that book can lead to errors if he narrates from memory. The accuracy of the book is a key component of a narrator's reliability. The example of Ibn Lahi'a is given: he was precise in his book, but after his books burned, his narrations became weak because he had relied on writing, not memory. The passage also notes that even temporary absence of books can have the same effect. This is an important principle in hadith criticism: a narrator's method of preservation matters, and changes in that method can affect reliability.

Key Terms
ضبط الكتاب (dabt al-kitab): The accuracy and reliability of a narrator's written record as a basis for narration.
ذهاب الكتب (dhahab al-kutub): Loss of a narrator's books, leading to reliance on fallible memory and potential errors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He lost his memory entirely.
B) He had relied on his books, not his memory, for accuracy.
C) He became blind.
D) He was exiled from Egypt.

Key Takeaway
Loss of a narrator's written records compromises reliability if the narrator depended on writing, not memory, leading to potential errors and scholarly disagreement.

Two Causes of Hadith Variation: Loss of Books and Lack of Precision
Explaining two causes of hadith variation: lost books and occasional imprecision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two causes of hadith variation discussed in the passage and explain how the loss of books can lead to errors in narration.

Core Idea
A narrator's reliance on written records is crucial; without them, even trustworthy narrators falter.

In this lesson, we explore two factors that can cause discrepancies in hadith transmission: the loss or unavailability of a narrator's written notes, and occasional lapses in precision among otherwise reliable narrators. We will examine classical examples of scholars whose narrations suffered when their books were lost or absent, and understand why this matters for hadith verification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two causes
Read the passage and note the two causes explicitly mentioned: (1) loss/absence of books, and (2) lack of precision. The second is introduced but not elaborated; the first is illustrated with examples.
2
Examine the example of Ibn Lahi'a
Ibn Lahi'a was a reliable narrator as long as he had his books. When they burned (around 169-170 AH), errors appeared in his narrations. This shows that his accuracy depended on his written records.
Imam Ahmad said he was better at reading his books than Ibn Wahb, but after the fire he became weak.
3
Examine the example of Ma'mar ibn Rashid
Ma'mar's narrations in Yemen (where his books were with him) were sound, but in Basra (where he narrated without his books) they were disordered. This illustrates the effect of narrating away from one's written notes.
Ya'qub ibn Shayba noted that the Basran narrations from Ma'mar contained disorder because his books were not with him.
4
Examine the example of Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash
Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash was trustworthy for Syrian narrations but unreliable for Hijazi ones because his book on Hijazi traditions was lost, causing him to mix up his memory.
Yahya ibn Ma'in explicitly distinguished between his Syrian and Hijazi narrations based on the availability of his book.
5
Understand the principle
The common thread: absence of written records leads to errors (wahm) and disagreements. This underscores the importance of verifying the conditions under which a narrator transmitted, especially for Hanafi usul.
The passage also briefly mentions lack of precision (ḍabṭ) as a separate cause, but does not elaborate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التمهيدي بيان ماهية الاختلاف

حفظهم عَبْد الله بن لهيعة، أبو عَبْد الرَّحْمن الحضرمي، الفقيه قاضي مصر، كَانَ متقناً لكتابه، قال الإمام أحمد : ابن لهيعة أجود قراءة لكتبه من ابن وهب . وَقَدْ كَانَ جل اعتماده في روايته عَلَى كتبه، فلما احترقت ضُعَف في الرِّوَايَة لكثرة ما وجد من الوهم والخطأ في روايته بَعْدَ ذهاب كتبه. قَالَ إسحاق بن عيسى الطباع: احترقت كتب ابن لهيعة سنة تسع وستين . وَقَالَ البخاري عَنْ يحيى بن بكير احترق منزل ابن لهيعة وكتبه في سنة سبعين ومئة . وربما يَكُون لغياب الكتب نَفْسُ أثرِ فَقْدِ الكتب ويكون مدعاة للوهم والخلاف، فإذا حدّث الرَّاوِي - الَّذِي يعتمد في الأداء عَلَى كتابه - في حالة غياب كتبه عنه، وقع الوهم والخطأ في حديثه، وتحديثه في غير بلده - أيضاً - مظنة لوقوع ذَلِكَ كَمَا حصل لمعمر بن راشد قَالَ ابن رجب: حديثه بالبصرة فيه اضطراب كثير، وحديثه باليمن جيد، وَقَالَ الإمام أحمد في رِوَايَة الأثرم: حَدِيث عَبد الرزاق عن معمر أحب إلي من حَدِيث هؤلاء البصريين كَانَ يتعاهد كتبه وينظر يعني باليمن، وَكَانَ يحدّثهم بخطأ بالبصرة . وَقَالَ يعقوب بن شيبة: سَمَاع أهل البصرة من معمر، حين قدم عليهم فيه اضطراب؛ لأن كتبه لَمْ تَكُنْ مَعَهُ . ومن هَؤُلاَءِ أَيْضاً: إسماعيل بن عياش قَالَ مُحَمَّد بن عثمان بن أبي شيبة: سَمِعْتُ يَحْيَى بن مَعِيْنِ يَقُول : إسماعيل بن عياش ثقة فِيْمَا رَوَى عَنْ الشاميين، وأما روايته عَنْ أهل الحجاز فإن كتابه ضاع فخلط في حفظه عَنْهُمْ.

سادساً. عدم الضبط: سبق الكلام أن الضبط من شروط صحة الحَدِيث الأساسية؛ ولكن بعض الرُّوَاة - وإن كانوا ضابطين - إلا أنهم في بعض الأحايين يخف ضبطهم لبعض الأحاديث
The introductory chapter: Explanation of the nature of difference. / 'Abdullah ibn Lahi'a, Abu 'Abd al-Rahman al-Hadrami, the jurist and judge of Egypt, was proficient in his book. Imam Ahmad said: Ibn Lahi'a is better at reading his books than Ibn Wahb. His main reliance in his narration was on his books. When they burned, he became weak in narration due to much error and mistake found in his narration after the loss of his books. Ishaq ibn 'Isa al-Tabba' said: Ibn Lahi'a's books burned in the year 169. Al-Bukhari said from Yahya ibn Bukayr: Ibn Lahi'a's house and books burned in the year 170. And perhaps the absence of books has the same effect as the loss of books, being a cause for error and disagreement. So when a narrator who relies on his book narrates without his books, error and mistake occur in his hadith. Also, narrating outside his own town is a likely place for that to happen, as happened with Ma'mar ibn Rashid. Ibn Rajab said: His hadith in Basra has much disorder, but his hadith in Yemen is good. Imam Ahmad narrated from al-Athram: The hadith of 'Abd al-Razzaq from Ma'mar is more beloved to me than the hadith of these Basrans; he used to maintain his books and look into them, meaning in Yemen, and he would narrate to them erroneously in Basra. Ya'qub ibn Shayba said: The hearing of the Basrans from Ma'mar when he came to them contains disorder because his books were not with him. Among these also is Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash. Muhammad ibn 'Uthman ibn Abi Shayba said: I heard Yahya ibn Ma'in say: Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash is trustworthy regarding what he narrated from Syrians, but as for his narration from the Hijazis, his book was lost so he mixed up in his memory about them. / Sixth: Lack of precision. It was previously mentioned that precision is one of the basic conditions for the authenticity of hadith. But some narrators, even though they are precise, occasionally their precision weakens for some hadiths.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage belongs to the introductory chapter on the nature of differences in hadith. It discusses two specific causes of variation: (1) the loss or absence of a narrator's written records, and (2) occasional lack of precision even among generally reliable narrators. The first cause is illustrated with three examples: Ibn Lahi'a, whose books burned, leading to errors; Ma'mar ibn Rashid, whose hadith were sound when narrated from his books in Yemen but disordered when he narrated from memory in Basra; and Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash, whose Syrian narrations are trustworthy but his Hijazi narrations suffered because his book was lost. The second cause is introduced but not elaborated here. The author emphasizes that reliance on written memory is crucial for accurate transmission, and its absence leads to mistakes and disagreement. This analysis is from a Hanafi usul perspective, where rigorous verification of narrators is essential for establishing reliable proofs.

Key Terms
ضبط (ḍabṭ): Precision and accuracy in narration, one of the essential conditions for hadith authenticity.
الوهم (al-wahm): Error or illusion in narration, occurring when a narrator relies on memory without written aids.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Ibn Lahi'a's reliability decline after his books burned?
A) He lost his memory entirely and could not narrate.
B) He relied on his books for accurate narration and without them made errors.
C) He became disinterested in hadith after the fire.
D) He moved to another city and no one trusted him.

Key Takeaway
The reliability of a hadith narrator is closely tied to the availability of his written records; loss or absence of those records can lead to errors and disagreements.

Tadlis as a Cause of Ikhtilaf among Hadith Transmitters
Analyzing tadlis as a cause of ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how tadlis in hadith chains can generate scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) and analyze how the presence of a mudallis narrator affects the authenticity of a hadith.

Core Idea
Tadlis omits a transmitter, causing chains to conflict and leading to ikhtilaf among scholars.

This passage examines a real case where tadlis created doubt about a hadith's authenticity. Ibn Jurayj, a known mudallis, narrated from Nafi' but actually heard it through a weak intermediary. By comparing the chains, scholars identified the problem. This illustrates how careful isnad analysis is crucial for usul al-fiqh, where the reliability of a hadith directly impacts legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudallis narrator
Ibn Jurayj is known to engage in tadlis. When he says 'from Nafi'', it is ambiguous: did he hear it directly? detect this as the first analytical step.
Mudallis narrators use terms like 'عن' to imply direct hearing.
2
Trace the alternative chain
Look for a more complete chain that reveals the missing link. In this case, the chain through Ibn Abi al-Mukhariq shows the actual intermediary.
Ibn Majah and others preserved the full chain.
3
Compare the conflicting reports
Note that the chain through Ibn Jurayj alone appears marfu' (raised to the Prophet), while the mawquf chain (from 'Ubaydullah) stops at 'Umar. The conflict arises because of the omitted link.
Al-Tirmidhi considered the mawquf version more authentic.
4
Conclude the effect on ikhtilaf
Because of tadlis, scholars disagree on which version is correct and whether the hadith can be used as evidence. Thus tadlis is a direct cause of ikhtilaf.
Imam al-Haramayn and others have discussed this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَكُون ابن جريج لَمْ يسمعه من نافع، وَقَدْ صح ظنُّه، فإنّ ابن جريج إما سمعه من ابن أبي المخارق كَمَا ثبت في رِوَايَة ابن ماجه هَذِهِ والحاكم في المستدرك واعتذر عَنْ تخريجه أنه إنّما أخرجه في المتابعات).وَقَالَ الترمذي: «إنما رفع هَذَا الْحَدِيث عَبْد الكريم بن أبي المخارق، وَهُوَ ضعيف عِنْدَ أهل الْحَدِيث ضعفه أيوب السختياني وتكلم فيه. وروى عبيد الله، عَنْ نافع عن ابن عمر قَالَ: قَالَ عمر ما بلتُ قائِماً منذ أسلَمْتُ. وهذا أصح من حَدِيث عبد الكريم).قَالَ: أقول: رِوَايَة عبيد الله الموقوفة أخرجها ابن أبي شيبة والبزار في مسنده من طريق عبيد الله بن عمر عَنْ نافع، عَنْ ابن عمر، عَنْ عمر موقوفاً، وَهُوَ الصواب. ومما يدل على عدم صحة حَدِيث ابن أبي المخارق أن الحافظ ابن حجر وَلَمْ يثبت عَنْ النَّبِي صلى الله عليه وسلم في النهي عنه شيء. بَعْدَ هَذَا العرض السريع بان لنا واتضح أن التدليس سبب من أسباب الاختلاف لدى الْمُحَدِّثِينَ؛ إِذْ إِنه قَدْ يسفر عن سقوط رجل من الإسناد فيخالف الرَّاوِي غيره من الرُّوَاة.
Ibn Jurayj did not hear it from Nafi', and his assumption was correct; Ibn Jurayj either heard it from Ibn Abi al-Mukhariq as confirmed by Ibn Majah's narration and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak, and he apologized for its inclusion by saying he only cited it in al-Mutaba'at. Al-Tirmidhi said: 'Only 'Abd al-Karim ibn Abi al-Mukhariq raised this hadith, and he is weak according to hadith scholars; Ayyub al-Sakhtiyani weakened him and spoke about him. 'Ubaydullah narrated from Nafi' from Ibn 'Umar who said: 'Umar said: I have not urinated standing since I embraced Islam. This is more authentic than the hadith of 'Abd al-Karim.' He said: I say: The mawquf narration of 'Ubaydullah was reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah and al-Bazzar in his Musnad via 'Ubaydullah ibn 'Umar from Nafi' from Ibn 'Umar from 'Umar as mawquf, and that is correct. Among the evidence for the unreliability of the hadith of Ibn Abi al-Mukhariq is that al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: 'Nothing authentic from the Prophet (peace be upon him) is established concerning the prohibition of it.' After this brief presentation, it became clear to us that tadlis is one of the causes of disagreement among hadith scholars; because it may result in the omission of a link in the chain, causing the narrator to differ from other narrators.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
The passage is an analysis of tadlis (deception in hadith transmission) as a cause of ikhtilaf (disagreement) among hadith scholars. The author presents a specific case: Ibn Jurayj's narration from Nafi' is suspected of being interrupted because he was known to engage in tadlis. Scholars found that Ibn Jurayj actually heard the hadith through a weak narrator, Ibn Abi al-Mukhariq, rather than directly from Nafi'. Al-Tirmidhi notes the weakness of Ibn Abi al-Mukhariq and cites a stronger narration from 'Ubaydullah as mawquf (stopped) to 'Umar. The author concludes that tadlis can lead to a missing link, causing a narrator to contradict others and thus creating divergence in ahadith. This demonstrates how critical chain analysis is in hadith studies.

Key Terms
التدليس (al-tadlis): Concealing a deficiency in the chain by using ambiguous terms like 'عن' (from) to imply direct hearing when there is an intermediary.
الإسناد (al-isnad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does tadlis cause ikhtilaf among hadith scholars?
A) By introducing a fabricated text
B) By causing a missing link in the chain, leading to contradictions with other chains
C) By contradicting the Quran
D) By changing the meaning of words

Key Takeaway
Tadlis is a key cause of ikhtilaf because the missing link creates apparent contradictions that scholars must resolve through chain analysis.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Preoccupation with Hadith as a Cause of Ikhtilaf
Explaining a cause of juristic disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the rigorous standards for hadith transmission contributed to ikhtilaf among jurists.

Core Idea
Extreme care for hadith created ikhtilaf because scholars set different thresholds for accepting narrators.

The noble prophetic hadith is the second source of Islamic legislation, and the early scholars exhibited extraordinary dedication to its preservation. However, this very diligence—writing by day, collating by night, and strict criteria for narrators—led to differences of opinion. When scholars disagreed on the reliability of a narrator or the authenticity of a report, their rulings varied accordingly. This lesson explores how the pursuit of authentic hadith itself became a source of ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the supreme status of hadith
Identify the Quranic verses and scholarly statements in the passage that establish hadith as a primary source of guidance and legislation.
This sets the context for why scholars devoted themselves to it.
2
Describe the rigorous methods of hadith scholars
Note the detailed activities mentioned: writing by day, collating by night, memorizing by day, discussing by night. Understand that this was the norm for the 'people of hadith'.
Such intense effort indicates the high stakes.
3
Analyze the consequence of these standards
From the examples of Abu al-Zinad (a hundred trustworthy men not taken from) and Malik ibn Anas (elders ignored in favor of Ibn Shihab), deduce that only those who met exceptional criteria were considered reliable narrators.
This illustrates that the bar for acceptance was very high and not uniform.
4
Link high standards to ikhtilaf
Explain how differing personal judgments of scholars on the reliability of narrators and the authenticity of reports directly led to variant fiqh rulings. The strictness itself generated disagreement.
This is the core causal link between hadith preoccupation and ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثامناً. الانشغال عَنْ الْحَدِيث: الْحَدِيث النبوي الشريف أحد المراجع الرئيسة للفقه الإسلامي، لذا كان علم الْحَدِيث رِوَايَة ودراية من أشرف العلوم وأجلها، بَلْ هُوَ أجلها عَلَى الإطلاق بَعْدَ العلم بالقرآن الكريم الَّذِي هُوَ أصل الدين ومنبع الطريق المستقيم، فالحديث هو المصدر الثاني للتشريع الإسلامي، بعضه يستقل بالتشريع، وكثيرٌ مِنْهُ شارح لكتاب الله تَعَالَى مبين لما جاء فِيْهِ. قَالَ تعالى ﴿ وَأَنزَلْنَا إِلَيْكَ الذِّكْرَ لِتُبَيِّنَ لِلنَّاسِ مَا نُزِلَ إِلَيْهِمْ ﴾ مِن هَذَا أدرك المسلمون أهمية الحَدِيث النبوي الشريف فعانوا ما عانوا من أجل حفظ الْحَدِيث النبوي الشريف فتخلوا عَنْ كُلّ شيء أمام هَذَا الهدف العزيز الغالي، وَهُوَ حَدِيث النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ تَعَالَى: ﴿ النَّبِيُّ أَوْلَى بِالْمُؤْمِنِينَ مِنْ أَنفُسِهِمْ ) . وللحرص الشديد على حفظ السنة اهتم المسلمون بمذاكرة الحَدِيث ومدارسته من أجل حفظه وضبطه وإتقانه، فكان الْمُحَدِّثُوْنَ يكتبون بالنهار ويعارضون بالليل ويحفظون بالنهار ويتذاكرون بالليل. وهكذا شأن الْمُحَدِّثِينَ، ومن لَمْ يَكُنْ كذلك فلا يسمى من أهل الْحَدِيث، وأسند الإمام مُسْلِم في مقدمة صحيحه عَنْ أبي الزناد قَالَ: «أدركت بالمدينة مئة، كلهم مأمونون ما يؤخذ عَنْهُمْ الْحَدِيْث يقال: ليس من أهله».وَقَالَ مالك بن أنس: أدركت مشايخ بالمدينة أبناء سبعين وثمانين لا يؤخذ عَنْهُمْ، ويقدم ابن شهاب وَهُوَ دونهم في السن فتزدحم الناس عَلَيْهِ».
Eighth: Preoccupation with Hadith. The noble prophetic hadith is one of the main references for Islamic jurisprudence, so the science of hadith, both narration and understanding, is among the noblest and most esteemed sciences; indeed it is the most esteemed absolutely after knowledge of the Noble Quran, which is the foundation of religion and the source of the straight path. Hadith is the second source of Islamic legislation; some of it stands alone in legislation, and much of it is an explanation of the Book of Allah, clarifying what is in it. Allah said: 'And We revealed to you the Reminder that you may make clear to the people what was sent down to them.' From this, Muslims realized the importance of the noble prophetic hadith, so they endured what they endured for the sake of preserving it, forsaking everything for this precious and dear goal, which is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Allah said: 'The Prophet is more worthy of the believers than themselves.' And due to the extreme care for preserving the Sunnah, Muslims devoted themselves to reviewing and studying hadith for its preservation, accuracy, and mastery. The hadith scholars would write by day and collate by night, memorize by day and discuss by night. Such was the way of hadith scholars; whoever was not like this was not considered among the people of hadith. Imam Muslim narrated in the introduction to his Sahih from Abu al-Zinad who said: 'I found in Medina a hundred people, all trustworthy, but hadith was not taken from them; it was said: He is not of its people.' And Malik ibn Anas said: 'I found in Medina elders of seventy and eighty from whom hadith was not taken, while Ibn Shihab, younger than them, was advanced and people crowded around him.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage discusses the eighth cause of ikhtilaf: preoccupation with hadith. The author emphasizes the supreme importance of hadith as the second source of legislation and the Muslim scholars' dedication to its preservation. This dedication led to rigorous standards for accepting narrators, as illustrated by the examples from Abu al-Zinad and Malik. Only those who met high standards of memorization, revision, and accuracy were considered 'people of hadith.' This scrutiny inadvertently contributed to differences because scholars varied in their acceptance criteria, leading to disagreements on which narrations to accept. The passage shows that the very effort to preserve hadith authentically could itself generate i!khtilaf.

Key Terms
الانشغال عن الحديث (al-inshighāl 'an al-hadīth): The intense preoccupation and dedication to preserving and transmitting hadith, leading to stringent standards.
مذاكرة الحديث (mudhākarat al-hadīth): Reviewing and discussing hadith for memorization and verification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the preoccupation with hadith preservation cause ikhtilaf?
A) It led to the loss of many authentic hadith
B) Strict acceptance criteria meant some narrations were accepted by some scholars but rejected by others
C) It caused scholars to ignore the Quran
D) It made hadith scholarship less important

Key Takeaway
The rigorous, subjective nature of hadith criticism was itself a source of juristic ikhtilaf.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

How Preoccupation with Judiciary and Fiqh Affects Hadith Narration
Explaining how judgeship and fiqh preoccupation affect hadith narration and cause differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a narrator's occupation with judiciary or fiqh can reduce their precision (ḍabṭ) and explain the implications for hadith authentication and juristic differences.

Core Idea
A narrator's professional preoccupation compromises their ḍabṭ, leading to unreliable hadith and causing juristic differences.

This passage explores why some narrators were considered less precise in hadith transmission. The author explains that important responsibilities like serving as a judge or devoting oneself to fiqh can divert time and attention away from reviewing and discussing hadith, resulting in memory lapses. This insight helps us understand why juristic differences (ikhtilāf) sometimes originate from variation in narrator reliability rather than textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factors discussed
The passage names preoccupation with judiciary and with fiqh as causes of decreased precision.
These are distinct but both consume time and mental focus.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Both roles demand significant time and mental focus, leaving less opportunity for hadith review and discussion, which are essential for maintaining ḍabṭ.
The author uses a cause-effect argument: engagement in these duties → less review → weaker memory.
3
Analyze the supporting examples
The author cites specific narrators (Sharik al-Nakha'i, Ibn Abi Layla, Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman) whose precision declined after taking up these roles.
Sharik's confusion began after becoming qadi; Hammad was praised for fiqh but criticized for hadith precision.
4
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Recognize that narrator precision is a condition for accepting a hadith; when compromised, the hadith may be considered weak, leading to differences in rulings derived from it.
This is a foundational principle: 'Amm al-riwayah requires 'adalah and dabt.
5
Appreciate the contribution of jarḥ wa taʿdīl
The passage shows how hadith critics considered these contextual factors when evaluating narrators.
This is a practical application of jarh wa ta'dil methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التمهيدي بيان ماهية الاختلاف

وهناك أمور جعلت عدداً من جهابذة الْمُحَدِّثِيْنَ لا يأخذون عن عدد كبير من أن هؤلاء الرُّوَاة كانوا يتشاغلون عَنْ الْحَدِيث والتشاغل عَنْ الْحَدِيث مدعاة لعدم ضبط الْحَدِيث وعدم إتقانه وربما كَانَ مآل ذَلِكَ إلى دخول بعض الوهم والعلل والاختلافات؛ لأن المذاكرة والمراجعة يعينان عَلَى ضبط الْحَدِيث وإتقانه. والانشغال في بعض الأمور ربما يحول دون المذاكرة والمراجعة مِمَّا يؤدي إلى عدم ضبط الروايات. ومن تِلْكَ الأمور:

أ. ولاية القضاء:

إن ولاية القضاء من الأمور الدينية المهمة والمجتمع الإسلامي بحاجة لازمة إلى هَذَا المنصب قَالَ تَعَالَى: ﴿ وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَوَةٌ يَتأُولِي الْأَلْبَبِ ﴾. ولمكانة هَذِهِ الوظيفة في الإسلام وأهميتها البالغة فالأمر يستدعي من القاضي توفيراً واسعاً لمزيد من الوقت، وتهيئة جوّ ملائم للقضاء؛ لأن القضاء مسؤولية دينية ودنيوية، وَقَدْ قَالَ : «مَنْ وُليَ القَضَاءَ، أو جُعِلَ قاضياً بَيْنَ النَّاسِ فَقَدْ ذُبِحَ بِغَيرِ سكين». إذن فهذه المسؤولية تستدعي تفرغاً وتفكراً ومراجعة، والحديث النبوي يحتاج كَذَلِكَ إِلَى تفرُّغ نسبي للمراجعة والمذاكرة من أجل الحفاظ على الضبط. وقد وجدنا حِيْنَ استقرأناً حال كَثِير من الرُّوَاة الَّذِيْنَ ولوا القضاء أنهم قد خفّ ضبطهم لانشغالهم بهذا المنصب الوظيفي، ومن أولئك شريك بن عَبْد الله النخعي الْقَاضِي، حدّد ابن حبّان تخليطه بَعْدَ عام خمسين ومئة حين تولى قضاء الكُوفَةِ). وكذلك مُحَمَّد بن عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن أبي لیلی) قَالَ أبو حاتم الرازي: «شغل بالقضاء فساء حفظه».

ب. الاشتغال بالفقه:

الفقه الإسلامي يمثل الشريعة الإسلامية الغراء وذلك لما احتواه من الأصول العظيمة الَّتِي تصلح لكل زمان ومكان والفقه الإسلامي واسع في أصوله وفروعه. ومن يشتغل بهذا العلم العظيم يحتاج إلى خلفيات بعدة علوم. وهذا يستدعي وقتاً واسعاً وتفرغاً كبيراً، ومن كَانَ الفقه أكبر همه ربما قصَّر في ضبط بعض أحاديثه؛ لأن ذَلِكَ ربما شغله عَنْ مراجعة حديثه. وكثير من الَّذِيْنَ يشتغلون بعلم من العلوم ويستفرغون العمر في تخصصهم يَكُون ذَلِكَ مدعاة للتقصير بالعلوم الأخرى. وَقَد وجدنا بعض جهابذة الْحَدِيث تَكَلَّمَ في بعض الرُّوَاةِ لِقَصْرِ تهممهم الفقه، ومن أولئك حماد بن أبي سليمان من كبار الفقهاء وشيخ أبي حَنِيفَة النعمان قَالَ عَنْهُ أبو إسحاق الشيباني: «ما رأيت أحداً أفقه من حماد). ومع هَذَا فَقَدْ نقل عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن أبي حاتم عَنْ أمير المؤمنين في الْحَدِيث شعبة بن الحجاج قوله: «كَانَ حماد - يعني: ابن أبي سليمان - لا يحفظ». ثُمَّ عقب ابن أبي حاتم عَلَى ذَلِكَ فَقَالَ: «يعني: إن الغالب عَلَيْهِ الفقه وإنه لَمْ يرزق حفظ الآثار». وَقَالَ أبو حاتم: «هُوَ صدوق ولا يحتج بحديثه، هُوَ مستقيم في الفقه، وإذا جاء الآثار شوّش».

وهنا وضع علماء الجرح والتعديل قواعد في أنّ الفقهاء غَيْرِ الْمُحَدِّثِيْنَ يغلب
The introductory chapter: Explanation of the nature of difference. And there are matters that caused a number of prominent hadith scholars not to accept from a large number of narrators because these narrators were occupied away from hadith, and being occupied away from hadith is a cause for lack of precision in hadith and lack of mastery, and perhaps the outcome of that leads to the entry of some illusion, defects, and differences. Because discussion and review help in precision and mastery of hadith. And engaging in some matters may prevent discussion and review, leading to lack of precision of narrations. Among those matters: A. Judiciary: Indeed, judiciary is among important religious matters, and the Islamic community is in necessary need of this position. Allah said: 'And there is for you in legal retribution life, O people of understanding.' And due to the status of this function in Islam and its great importance, the matter requires the judge to allocate ample time and prepare a suitable environment for judging, because judgment is a religious and worldly responsibility. And the Prophet said: 'Whoever is appointed as a judge or made a judge among people has been slaughtered without a knife.' So this responsibility requires devotion, reflection, and review, and the prophetic hadith likewise requires relative devotion for review and discussion in order to preserve precision. And we found when we surveyed the condition of many narrators who undertook judiciary that their precision decreased due to their preoccupation with this official position. Among them is Sharik bin Abdullah al-Nakha'i the judge; Ibn Hibban specified that his confusion began after the year one hundred and fifty when he undertook the judiciary of Kufa. Likewise, Muhammad bin Abd al-Rahman bin Abi Layla; Abu Hatim al-Razi said: 'He was busied with judiciary, so his memory deteriorated.' B. Preoccupation with fiqh: Islamic fiqh represents the shining Islamic law due to its containing great principles that are suitable for every time and place, and Islamic fiqh is vast in its principles and branches. Whoever engages in this great science needs backgrounds in several sciences, and this requires ample time and great devotion. And whoever's greatest concern is fiqh, perhaps he falls short in precision of some of his hadiths, because that may occupy him from reviewing his hadith. And many of those who engage in a science and spend their lifetime in their specialization become a cause for deficiency in other sciences. And we found that some prominent hadith scholars spoke about some narrators due to their focus on fiqh. Among them is Hammad bin Abi Sulayman, one of the great jurists and the teacher of Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man. Abu Ishaq al-Shaybani said about him: 'I have not seen anyone more knowledgeable in fiqh than Hammad.' Yet Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Hatim narrated from the Commander of the Faithful in Hadith, Shu'ba ibn al-Hajjaj, his statement: 'Hammad—meaning Ibn Abi Sulayman—did not memorize.' Then Ibn Abi Hatim commented on that saying: 'He means that fiqh predominates over him and he was not granted the preservation of traditions.' And Abu Hatim said: 'He is truthful but his hadith is not used as evidence; he is upright in fiqh, but when it comes to traditions he becomes confused.' Here the scholars of jarh and ta'dil laid down rules that jurists who are not hadith scholars predominate...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is from the introductory chapter, explaining the nature of differences in hadith and fiqh. The author identifies two key factors that lead to imprecision in hadith narration, which in turn cause juristic differences: preoccupation with judiciary and preoccupation with fiqh. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that these responsibilities consume time and attention that would otherwise be spent on reviewing and discussing hadith, leading to lapses in memory and precision. The author provides examples: Sharik al-Nakha'i and Ibn Abi Layla became less precise after becoming judges; Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman, though a great jurist and teacher of Abu Hanifa, was criticized for weak hadith preservation. The passage underscores that differences in fiqh can arise not only from textual interpretation but also from the varying reliability of narrators due to their professional engagements. This is a key insight in usul: the authority of a narration depends on the narrator's precision (dabt), which can be compromised by distractions. The author concludes that scholars of jarh wa ta'dil formulated principles about jurists who are not specialists in hadith, implying that their hadith may be less reliable.

Key Terms
الجَرح والتعديل (al-jarḥ wa al-taʿdīl): The science of criticizing and authenticating hadith narrators, evaluating their honesty and precision.
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilāf): Disagreement or difference, specifically juristic differences arising from variant hadith narrations.
ضبط (ḍabṭ): Precision in memorization and transmission of hadith; a key condition for narrator reliability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Preoccupation with judiciary
C) Preoccupation with travel
D) Preoccupation with farming

Key Takeaway
A narrator's professional preoccupations can compromise their precision in hadith, and this is a recognized cause of juristic differences.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
beginner
8 min
The Difficulty of Uncovering Differences in Hadith Chains and Texts
Introduces the importance and difficulty of ikhtilaf studies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prerequisites for studying differences in hadith and explain why this field is considered extremely difficult.

Core Idea
Uncovering differences in hadith requires deep knowledge, extensive practice, and a special gift from Allah.

This lesson introduces the critical field of ikhtilaf (differences) in hadith, which is essential for Usul al-Fiqh when resolving conflicting narrations. Scholars like Imam al-Shafi'i and others authored dedicated works, but the task is far from simple. The passage highlights the immense difficulty and the rigorous qualifications needed to identify errors in chains (isnad) and texts (matn). As future Hanafi jurists, understanding this difficulty will help you appreciate the care required in deriving rulings from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scholarly tradition
List the scholars mentioned: Imam al-Shafi'i, Ibn Qutaybah, al-Saji, al-Tahawi, Ibn al-Jawzi. Recognize that they authored works specifically on ikhtilaf al-hadith, showing the importance of this field.
These works contain both textual and chain differences.
2
Understand the author's assertion of difficulty
The passage states uncovering differences is not easy but extremely difficult. Note the phrases 'shāqqun bil-ghāyah' (very difficult) and the conditions of 'fahman wāsi'an wa ittilā'an kabīran' (broad understanding and vast knowledge).
3
Analyze the quotes from Ibn Hajar and Ibn Rajab
Ibn Hajar calls this field 'the most obscure and subtle'. Ibn Rajab emphasizes lengthy practice and frequent discussion. Identify the specific prerequisites each mentions.
Ibn Rajab: 'fahman khāssan' (special understanding) developed through practice.
4
Synthesize the required qualifications
From the passage, list: knowledge of narrators, chains, methods of transmission, familiarity with works of earlier critics, ability to distinguish authentic from weak chains, and understanding the basis for preference (tarjih).
These are the tools of a hadith critic (nāqid).
5
Apply the first methodological point
The passage introduces knowing the prolific narrators around whom chains revolve. Reflect on why this is beneficial: it helps trace how hadiths spread and become mixed, aiding in error detection.
This is the start of a longer methodology outlined in subsequent sections.

Introductory chapter: Explanation of the nature of difference. Then we observe that scholars of fiqh and hadith have compiled comprehensive books in which they addressed the differences and excelled; thus we find that Imam al-Shafi'i authored 'Ikhtilaf al-Hadith', then he was followed by Ibn Qutaybah, Abu Yahya Zakariyya ibn Yahya al-Saji, al-Tahawi, and Ibn al-Jawzi. These books contain differences of texts and chains, and they are serious scientific studies with few parallels, showing us the concern of muhaddithin for both the fiqh and hadith aspects, and knowing the differences of these two sciences protects its possessor from error and saves him from illusion. Chapter Seven: Uncovering the difference. Uncovering the hadith differences occurring in chains and texts is not an easy and simple matter; rather it is extremely difficult, and only those whom Allah has granted broad understanding and vast knowledge can manage it. Knowing the differences occurring in texts and chains cannot be reached except through paths and contemplation with complete knowledge of the narrators, teachers, and students, how students received from teachers, circumstances, events, methods of transmission, and methods of performance in order to know correctness and how error and mistake occur in narration. This requires strenuous effort. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: 'This art is the most obscure of the types of hadith and the most subtle in approach, and it can only be undertaken by one whom Allah the Exalted has granted deep understanding, comprehensive knowledge, awareness of the ranks of narrators, and penetrating insight.' Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali said: 'The skilled critics among the memorizers, due to their frequent practice with hadith, their knowledge of men and the hadiths of each one of them, have a special understanding by which they know that this hadith resembles the hadith of so-and-so and does not resemble the hadith of so-and-so, and thus they declare hadiths as defective.' It is required of one who speaks about defects and uncovers differences in texts and chains that he be well-versed in narrations, familiar with books, extensive in research, and a frequent examiner. Hence Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali said: 'In this science, lengthy practice and frequent discussion are necessary; if discussion is lacking, then let the seeker frequently read the words of the knowledgeable imams like Yahya al-Qattan, and those who learned from him like Ahmad and Ibn al-Madini and others. Whoever is granted the study of that, understands it, his soul becomes perceptive in it, and he gains psychological strength and competence in it, then he is qualified to speak in it.' It is also required of one who wishes to uncover hadith differences that he know the authentic and weak chains, the trustworthy narrators who were weakened because of some of their teachers, and the trustworthy narrators whose hadiths are strengthened by their narration from certain teachers; because this is the basis of preference and by it one knows to identify error from the correct. It is possible to outline points through which we perceive differences, whether in texts or in chains, by which illusion and differences can be uncovered, and how to deal with that as authentication or weakening, as follows: First: Knowing the narrators around whom the chain revolves. Knowing the narrators around whom the chain revolves—the prolific narrators who have many students and multiple hadith schools—has great benefit for the hadith critic whose concern is knowledge.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an introductory discussion on the nature of ikhtilaf (difference) in hadith, setting the stage for a detailed study. The author begins by noting that scholars of both fiqh and hadith have authored comprehensive works on differences, citing Imam al-Shafi'i's 'Ikhtilaf al-Hadith' and subsequent works by Ibn Qutaybah, al-Saji, al-Tahawi, and Ibn al-Jawzi. These works are described as serious scientific studies that highlight the scholars' concern with both the legal and hadith aspects, and they protect the reader from error. The author then emphasizes that uncovering differences in chains (asanid) and texts (mutun) is extremely difficult and requires deep understanding and vast knowledge. He quotes Ibn Hajar and Ibn Rajab to reinforce this point. Ibn Hajar states that this field is the most obscure and subtle, requiring a special gift from Allah. Ibn Rajab explains that skilled critics develop a special understanding through practice and knowledge of narrators. The author then outlines prerequisites for one who wishes to comment on defects and uncover differences: comprehensive knowledge of narrations, extensive reading, frequent discussion, and familiarity with the words of earlier imams like Yahya al-Qattan, Ahmad, and Ibn al-Madini. He also emphasizes the need to know authentic and weak chains, and the status of trustworthy narrators. Finally, he introduces the first point of a methodology: knowing the prolific narrators around whom chains revolve, as this helps the critic differentiate between correct and erroneous narrations. This passage functions as a front matter, explaining the importance and difficulty of the subject, and preparing the reader for the subsequent detailed analysis.

Key Terms
اختلاف (ikhtilaf): Difference or disagreement in hadith texts and chains, as studied by scholars to identify correct narrations.
إسناد (isnad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith; the passage emphasizes that differences occur in both the chain and the text.
متن (matn): The text or content of a hadith; differences in the text are a key focus of the study.

Key Takeaway
The study of differences in hadith is a demanding field requiring comprehensive knowledge, practice, and divine gift; it is foundational for reliable fiqh derivation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Disagreement in Hadith: Harmful vs Harmless
Defines and distinguishes harmful from harmless disagreement in hadith chains and texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between detrimental (qadih) and non-detrimental disagreements in hadith and state the positions of the group and the critical hadith scholars regarding narrators naming two different individuals.

Core Idea
Non-detrimental disagreement is harmless, but inconsistency in naming different narrators indicates lack of precision and harms the hadith.

Hadith scholars examine disagreements in chains and texts. Some disagreements are harmless (e.g., different wordings with same meaning), while others indicate a flaw. This lesson classifies these types and presents a key dispute: when a narrator names two different people in different versions of a hadith, is it harmful if both are trustworthy?

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disagreement
Determine if the disagreement is a diversity of expression (ikhtilaf tanawwu') or a genuine contradiction (ikhtilaf tadadd). The former is harmless.
If the same meaning can be expressed in different words, it is not qadih.
2
Check if reconciliation is possible
If the apparent disagreement can be reconciled—e.g., one version says 'from a man' and the other names him—there is no harm.
The named man could be the same as the ambiguous one.
3
Analyze naming of two different individuals
When a narrator names a specific person in one version and a different person in another, this causes pause. Two possibilities: the hadith is from both, or the narrator is inconsistent about who he heard from.
This is the critical case discussed in the passage.
4
Apply trustworthiness
If both named individuals are trustworthy, a group considers the disagreement harmless (either goes to a reliable narrator). The critical scholars (jahabidhah) see it as detrimental because it shows lack of precision (dabt).
Precision is a condition for hadith authenticity.
5
Consider evidence of joint transmission
If there is evidence that the narrator heard from both (e.g., a third version combines them), then no problem. If one is weak, the hadith is unreliable.
The author seems to lean toward the stricter view unless evidence resolves the inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثامن الاختلاف القادح والاختلاف غير القادح مِمَّا لا شك فيه أن الاختلاف غير القادح لا عبرة بِهِ ولا أثر لَهُ عِنْدَ الْمُحَدِّثِينَ، ونحن حينما عنينا بدراسة هَذِهِ الاختلافات في الأسانيد والمتون إنما قصدنا القادح مِنْهَا. واختلاف الرُّوَاة في أمر لا تناقض فِيْهِ لا يضر لأنه اختلاف تنوع لا اختلاف تضاد. إِذْ قَدْ يَكُون الاختلاف بَيْنَ طريقين أحدهما قوي والآخر ضعيف فمثل هَذَا الاختلاف لا يقدح؛ لأن الرّوَايَة الصحيحة لا تقدح بها الرّوَايَة الضعيفة ولا تؤثر. وكذلك قَدْ يَكُون هناك اختلاف في الظاهر لكن يمكن الجمع بينهما؛ بحيث يمكن أن يَكُون المتكلم معبراً باللفظين الواردين عن معنى واحد، فلا إشكال أيضاً. مثل: أن يَكُون في أحد الوجهين قَدْ قَالَ الرَّاوِي: «عن رجل»، وفي الوجه الآخر سمى الرجل. هَذَا ويمكن أن يَكُون ذَلِكَ المسمى هُوَ ذَلِكَ المبهم، فلا تعارض. أما إذا سمى الرَّاوِي باسم معين في رِوَايَة، ويسميه باسم آخر في رِوَايَة أخرى فهذا محل توقف ونظر، إذ يتعارض فيه أمران: أحدهما: أنه يجوز أن يَكُون الحَدِيث عن الرجلين معاً. والثاني: أن يغلب عَلَى الظن أن الرَّاوِي واحد اختلف فيه. فهاهنا لا يخلو أن يَكُون الرجلان معاً ثقتين أو لا. فإن كانا ثقتين فعلى رأي جَمَاعَة لا يضر هَذَا الاختلاف؛ لأنه إن كَانَ الْحَدِيث عن هَذَا المعين فهو عدل، وإن كَانَ عن الآخر فهو عدل، فكيف انقلب الْحَدِيث فإلى عدل فلا يضر هَذَا الاختلاف بينما يرى جهابذة الْمُحَدِّثِينَ أَن هَذَا قادح في الرِّوَايَة إِذْ إنه يدل عَلَى عدم ضبط راويه لَهُ. والضبط شرط لصحة الْحَدِيث. وهذا إنما يتجه إذا كَانَ لا دليل لنا عَلَى أَنَّ الْحَدِيث عَنْهُما جميعاً. أما إن دل دليل فلا اختلاف مثل أن يروي إنسان حديثاً عن رجل تارة، ويروي ذَلِكَ الْحَدِيث عن آخر تارة ثُمَّ يرويه عَنْهُمَا معاً في مرة ثالثة. وأما إن كَانَ أحد الراويين ضعيفاً فَقَدْ تردد الحال بَيْنَ أَن يَكُون عن القوي أَوْ عن الضعيف أَوْ عَنْهُمَا، وَهُوَ عَلَى أحد هَذِهِ التقديرات غَيْر حجة، ثُمَّ إِن هَذَا يشترط فِيْهِ أن لا يَكُون الطريقان مختلفين بَلْ يكونان عن رجل واحد. ومع ذَلِكَ فيجوز أن يَكُون رَوَاهُ
Eighth Section: The detrimental disagreement and the non-detrimental disagreement. There is no doubt that non-detrimental disagreement is of no consequence and has no effect according to hadith scholars, and when we undertook the study of these disagreements in chains and texts, we intended only the detrimental ones. The disagreement of narrators in a matter where there is no contradiction does not harm because it is a diversity of expression, not a contradiction of opposition. For there may be disagreement between two chains, one strong and the other weak, and such disagreement is not detrimental because the authentic narration is not harmed by the weak one nor does it affect it. Likewise, there may be apparent disagreement but reconciliation is possible, such that the speaker may be expressing the same meaning with both wordings, so there is no problem. For example: in one version the narrator says 'from a man', and in the other version he names the man. This can be that the named person is the same as the ambiguous one, so no contradiction. But if the narrator names a specific person in one narration and names a different person in another narration, then this is a point of pause and scrutiny, since two matters conflict: one: it is possible that the hadith is from both men together. The second: it is most likely that the narrator is one and there is disagreement about him. Here it is inevitable that either both men are trustworthy or not. If both are trustworthy, then according to a group this disagreement does not harm; because if the hadith is from this specific one, he is reliable, and if it is from the other, he is reliable, so no matter how the hadith turns, it goes to a reliable narrator, so this disagreement does not harm. Meanwhile, the critical hadith scholars see that this is detrimental to the narration because it indicates lack of precision (dabt) of its narrator. And precision is a condition for the authenticity of the hadith. This applies if we have no evidence that the hadith is from both of them. But if evidence indicates, then there is no disagreement, such as when a person narrates a hadith from a man at one time, then narrates the same hadith from another at another time, then narrates it from both together a third time. But if one of the two narrators is weak, then the situation is uncertain between being from the strong or the weak or both, and in any of these estimates it is not an authority. Furthermore, this is conditioned that the two chains are not different but are from one man. Nevertheless, it is permissible that he narrated it...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage classifies disagreement in hadith into detrimental (qadih) and non-detrimental (ghayr qadih). Non-detrimental includes: (1) diversity of expression without contradiction (ikhtilaf tanawwu'), (2) one strong and one weak chain (the weak does not harm the strong), and (3) apparent disagreement that can be reconciled, such as when a narrator says 'from a man' in one version and names him in another. The crucial case is when a narrator names two different individuals in different versions. Here the author presents two views: a group considers it harmless if both are trustworthy, but critical hadith scholars (jahabidhah) consider it detrimental because it shows lack of precision (dabt) in the narrator. Precision is a necessary condition for hadith authenticity. The passage also discusses exceptions: if evidence shows the hadith is from both (e.g., the narrator combines them in a third version), then no problem. If one is weak, the hadith becomes unreliable. The author also stipulates that the two chains must be from the same single narrator for this issue to arise.

Key Terms
القادح (al-qādiḥ): A disagreement that harms the authenticity of the hadith because it implies contradiction or lack of precision.
الضبط (al-ḍabṭ): Precision in narration: the narrator's ability to accurately remember and transmit the hadith without error.
الاختلاف غير القادح (al-ikhtilāf ghayr al-qādiḥ): Non-detrimental disagreement that does not harm the hadith, such as diversity of expression or reconcilable differences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a narrator names two different trustworthy individuals in two versions of the same hadith, what is the view of the critical hadith scholars (jahabidhah)?
A) It does not harm because both are trustworthy
B) It is detrimental because it shows lack of precision
C) The hadith is automatically rejected
D) The narrator must be a liar

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, inconsistency in a narrator's chain, even when named individuals are trustworthy, may indicate weak dabt and render the hadith unreliable unless evidence resolves the discrepancy.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
15 min
Distinguishing Defective from Non-Defective Differences
Defines and classifies defective vs non-defective differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given difference in hadith as defective or non-defective and explain the conditions under which a naming variation is considered harmful.

Core Idea
A difference harms authenticity only if it indicates lack of narrator precision and cannot be resolved.

In Hadith studies, not every difference between narrations is damaging. This lesson introduces the key distinction between defective and non-defective differences, focusing on when a difference in naming narrators becomes a problem. Understanding this is crucial for evaluating hadith authenticity and the juristic rulings derived from them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of difference
Determine whether the difference involves a contradiction (tanāquḍ) or is merely a variety (tanawwuʿ). Non-defective differences include those that can be reconciled or where one chain is clearly stronger.
A difference in wording without semantic contradiction is not harmful.
2
Check for possibility of reconciliation
If the two narrations can be harmonized (e.g., one specifies what the other leaves general), then the difference is non-defective.
Example: 'from a man' vs. naming the man – they may refer to the same person.
3
Analyze naming variations
If the narrator names different individuals in different chains, assess whether both are trustworthy and whether there is evidence the hadith is from both.
If both are trustworthy and no evidence of dual transmission, the variation indicates lack of dabṭ and is defective.
4
Apply the condition of dabṭ
The leading hadith experts (jihābadha) consider such variation defective because precise memory (ḍabṭ) is a condition for authenticity. If the narrator cannot consistently name the source, his reliability is compromised.
The Hanafi school follows this view.
5
Conclude on defectiveness
If the variation cannot be resolved by reconciliation or preponderance, the hadith is weakened or rejected. If resolution is possible, the difference is non-defective.
True disruption (iḍṭirāb) requires equal strength of conflicting reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التمهيدي بيان ماهية الاختلاف المبحث الثامن الاختلاف القادح والاختلاف غير القادح مِمَّا لا شك فيه أن الاختلاف غير القادح لا عبرة بِهِ ولا أثر لَهُ عِنْدَ الْمُحَدِّثِينَ، ونحن حينما عنينا بدراسة هَذِهِ الاختلافات في الأسانيد والمتون إنما قصدنا القادح مِنْهَا. واختلاف الرُّوَاة في أمر لا تناقض فِيْهِ لا يضر لأنه اختلاف تنوع لا اختلاف تضاد. إِذْ قَدْ يَكُون الاختلاف بَيْنَ طريقين أحدهما قوي والآخر ضعيف فمثل هَذَا الاختلاف لا يقدح؛ لأن الرّوَايَة الصحيحة لا تقدح بها الرّوَايَة الضعيفة ولا تؤثر. وكذلك قَدْ يَكُون هناك اختلاف في الظاهر لكن يمكن الجمع بينهما؛ بحيث يمكن أن يَكُون المتكلم معبراً باللفظين الواردين عن معنى واحد، فلا إشكال أيضاً. مثل: أن يَكُون في أحد الوجهين قَدْ قَالَ الرَّاوِي: «عن رجل»، وفي الوجه الآخر سمى الرجل. هَذَا ويمكن أن يَكُون ذَلِكَ المسمى هُوَ ذَلِكَ المبهم، فلا تعارض. أما إذا سمى الرَّاوِي باسم معين في رِوَايَة، ويسميه باسم آخر في رِوَايَة أخرى فهذا محل توقف ونظر، إذ يتعارض فيه أمران: أحدهما: أنه يجوز أن يَكُون الحَدِيث عن الرجلين معاً. والثاني: أن يغلب عَلَى الظن أن الرَّاوِي واحد اختلف فيه. فهاهنا لا يخلو أن يَكُون الرجلان معاً ثقتين أو لا. فإن كانا ثقتين فعلى رأي جَمَاعَة لا يضر هَذَا الاختلاف؛ لأنه إن كَانَ الْحَدِيث عن هَذَا المعين فهو عدل، وإن كَانَ عن الآخر فهو عدل، فكيف انقلب الْحَدِيث فإلى عدل فلا يضر هَذَا الاختلاف بينما يرى جهابذة الْمُحَدِّثِينَ أَن هَذَا قادح في الرِّوَايَة إِذْ إنه يدل عَلَى عدم ضبط راويه لَهُ. والضبط شرط لصحة الْحَدِيث. وهذا إنما يتجه إذا كَانَ لا دليل لنا عَلَى أَنَّ الْحَدِيث عَنْهُما جميعاً. أما إن دل دليل فلا اختلاف مثل أن يروي إنسان حديثاً عن رجل تارة، ويروي ذَلِكَ الْحَدِيث عن آخر تارة ثُمَّ يرويه عَنْهُمَا معاً في مرة ثالثة. وأما إن كَانَ أحد الراويين ضعيفاً فَقَدْ تردد الحال بَيْنَ أَن يَكُون عن القوي أَوْ عن الضعيف أَوْ عَنْهُمَا، وَهُوَ عَلَى أحد هَذِهِ التقديرات غَيْر حجة، ثُمَّ إِن هَذَا يشترط فِيْهِ أن لا يَكُون الطريقان مختلفين بَلْ يكونان عن رجل واحد. ومع ذَلِكَ فيجوز أن يَكُون رَوَاهُ عَنْهُمَا جميعاً. وَقَدْ أشار الحافظ السيوطي في " التدريب إلى بعض الاختلافات غير القادحة بصحة الْحَدِيث عِنْدَ الْمُحَدِّثِينَ، قَالَ : فمن ذَلِكَ أنهما أخرجا قصة جمل جابر من طرق، وفيها اختلاف كثير في مقدار الثمن، وفي اشتراط ركوبه. وَقَدْ رجّح البخاري الطرق الَّتِي فِيْهَا الاشتراط على غيرها مع تخريج الأمرين، ورجح أيضاً كون الثمن أوقية مع تخريجه ما يخالف ذَلِكَ». قلت والاختلاف في ثمن البعير أنه جاء بأوقية وفي رِوَايَة بأربعة دنانير، وَهُوَ يَكُون بأوقية على حساب الدينار بعشرة دراهم. وفي رِوَايَة أوقية ذهب، وفي رِوَايَة ومئتي درهم وفي رِوَايَة أربع أواقٍ، وفي رِوَايَة بعشرين ديناراً. وَقَدْ خرّجها البخاري جميعها ورجح أنه بأوقية، قال البخاري: وقول الشعبي بأوقية أكثر الاشتراط أكثر وأصح عندي. وَقَدْ فسّر الحافظ ابن حجر ذَلِكَ بقوله: «أي أكثر طرقاً وأصح مخرجاً، ثُمَّ قَالَ: «وما جنح إِلَيْهِ المصنف من ترجيح رِوَايَة الاشتراط هُوَ الجاري عَلَى طريقة المحققين من أهل الْحَدِيث لأنهم لا يتوقفون عن تصحيح الْحَدِيث إذا وقع فيه الاختلاف إلا إذا تكافأت الروايات، وَهُوَ شرط الاضطراب الَّذِي يرد بِهِ الخبر، وَهُوَ مفقود هنا مع إمكان الترجيح).
The introductory chapter: Statement of the nature of difference. The eighth topic: Defective difference and non-defective difference. There is no doubt that non-defective difference is not considered and has no effect according to hadith scholars, and when we intended to study these differences in chains and texts, we only meant the defective among them. The difference of narrators in a matter in which there is no contradiction is not harmful because it is a difference of variety, not a difference of contradiction. Sometimes the difference may be between two chains, one strong and the other weak, so such a difference does not defect because the correct narration is not defected by the weak narration nor affected. Likewise, there may be an apparent difference, but reconciliation between them is possible, such that the speaker may have expressed one meaning with the two wordings, so there is no problem. For example: in one version the narrator said 'from a man', and in the other version he named the man. This, and it is possible that the named person is the same as the unspecified one, so no contradiction. But if the narrator names a specific person in one narration and names another person in another narration, then this is a place of pause and scrutiny, because two matters conflict: one: it is possible that the hadith is from both men together. The second: that it is most likely that the narrator is one person about whom there is difference. So in this case, it is not free from either both men being trustworthy or not. If both are trustworthy, then in the view of a group this difference does not harm; because whether the hadith is from this specific one, he is reliable, or from the other, he is reliable, so however the hadith turns, it goes to a reliable person, so this difference does not harm. However, the experts among hadith scholars see that this is defective in the narration because it indicates the lack of precision (dabt) of its narrator. And precision is a condition for the authenticity of hadith. And this is only applicable if we have no evidence that the hadith is from both of them. But if evidence indicates, then there is no difference, such as a person narrating a hadith from one man sometimes, then narrating that same hadith from another sometimes, and then narrating it from both together a third time. And if one of the two narrators is weak, then the situation vacillates between being from the strong, or from the weak, or from both, and upon any of these estimates it is not a proof. Then, this requires that the two chains not be different but rather be from one man. Nevertheless, it is possible that he narrated it from both of them. And al-Hafiz al-Suyuti in al-Tadrib pointed to some non-defective differences regarding the authenticity of hadith according to hadith scholars. He said: 'Among that is that they both (Bukhari and Muslim) brought out the story of Jabir's camel from multiple chains, and in them there is much difference in the amount of the price and in the condition of riding it. Al-Bukhari preferred the chains that contain the condition over others while transmitting both matters, and also preferred that the price was an uqiya while transmitting what contradicts that.' I say: And the difference in the price of the camel is that it came as an uqiya, and in a narration as four dinars, and it is an uqiya according to the calculation of the dinar as ten dirhams, and in a narration an uqiya of gold, and in a narration two hundred dirhams, and in a narration four awaq, and in a narration twenty dinars. Al-Bukhari transmitted all of them and preferred that it was an uqiya. Al-Bukhari said: 'The statement of al-Sha'bi that it was an uqiya, the condition is more numerous and more authentic in my view.' And al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar interpreted that by saying: 'i.e., more numerous in chains and more authentic in source.' Then he said: 'And what the author inclined to of preferring the narration with the condition is the practice according to the method of the verifiers among hadith scholars, because they do not hesitate to authenticate a hadith when difference occurs in it unless the narrations are equally matched, which is the condition of disruption (idhtirab) by which the report is rejected, and it is lacking here with the possibility of preponderance.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the classification of differences (ikhtilaf) in hadith into two types: defective (qadih) and non-defective (ghayr qadih). The author opens by stating that non-defective differences are disregarded by hadith scholars and have no effect. The study is concerned only with defective differences. A difference that does not entail contradiction (tanāquḍ) is not harmful; it is a difference of variety (tanawwuʿ), not of contradiction (taḍād). Examples: a strong chain versus a weak chain—the weak does not impair the strong; or an apparent difference that can be reconciled, such as one narrator saying 'from a man' and another naming the same man. A problematic case arises when a narrator names different individuals in different narrations. Two possibilities exist: the hadith might be from both men, or the narrator is one person but there is confusion. If both are trustworthy, some scholars deem it harmless because either way the narrator is reliable. However, the leading hadith experts (jihābadha) consider this defective because it indicates a lack of precise memory (ḍabṭ), a condition for authenticity. If there is evidence that the hadith is from both (e.g., the narrator reports it from each separately and then from both together), then no problem. If one narrator is weak, the narration is not a proof. The passage then cites al-Suyuti's example from the story of Jabir's camel: multiple narrations differ on the price and on whether riding the camel was a condition. Al-Bukhari included all versions but preferred the one mentioning the condition and the price of an uqiya, because those narrations are more numerous and more authentic. Ibn Hajar explains that hadith scholars do not reject a hadith due to such differences as long as one narration can be preferred; true disruption (iḍṭirāb) requires equal strength of conflicting narrations, which is absent here. The author thus demonstrates that many differences in hadith are non-defective and do not affect the ruling derived from the hadith.

Key Terms
الاختلاف القادح (al-ikhtilāf al-qādiḥ): الاختلاف الذي يضر بصحة الحديث بحيث لا يمكن الجمع أو الترجيح
الاختلاف غير القادح (al-ikhtilāf ghayr al-qādiḥ): الاختلاف الذي لا يؤثر في صحة الحديث، إما لعدم التناقض أو لإمكان الجمع أو الترجيح
الضبط (al-ḍabṭ): حفظ الراوي للحديث وضبطه له من غير نسيان أو خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what makes a naming variation in hadith defective?
A) The named narrators are not trustworthy.
B) The variation indicates lack of dabṭ in the reporter.
C) The hadith contradicts the Qur'an.
D) The chains are from different countries.

Key Takeaway
A difference in naming narrators is defective unless there is evidence of dual transmission, because it undermines the reporter's dabṭ.

Applying the Principle: The Case of Jabir's Camel
Applies the rule to a real case study

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the criteria for defective vs non-defective differences to a real hadith case and explain why al-Bukhari accepted multiple versions of the same event.

Core Idea
Multiple differing reports on a single event can coexist if one version can be preferred.

The story of Jabir's camel sale is narrated in multiple versions with differing prices and conditions. This lesson examines how al-Bukhari handled these variations and why they are considered non-defective. Understanding this case reinforces the principles of reconciliation and preponderance (tarjīḥ) in hadith criticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiple versions
List the different narrations: prices mentioned include one uqiya, four dinars, two hundred dirhams, four awaq, twenty dinars, and also variation on whether riding the camel was a condition of the sale.
All versions are from the same event (Jabir's sale of his camel to the Prophet).
2
Assess if reconciliation is possible
Consider whether the different numbers can be harmonized. For example, an uqiya equals forty dirhams, four dinars equals forty dirhams (if a dinar is ten dirhams), so some variations may be equivalent.
Some differences are merely different units of measurement for the same amount.
3
Apply tarjīḥ (preponderance)
Al-Bukhari compared the chains and preferred the version mentioning the condition of riding because it had more numerous and more authentic chains. He also preferred the price of one uqiya.
Tarjīḥ is used when reconciliation is not fully possible or to choose the most reliable report.
4
Conclude on defectiveness
Since the variations can be reconciled or one version can be preferred, the differences are non-defective. The hadith remains authentic.
True disruption (iḍṭirāb) would require equal strength of conflicting reports, which is absent here.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عَلَى الظن أن الرَّاوِي واحد اختلف فيه. فهاهنا لا يخلو أن يَكُون الرجلان معاً ثقتين أو لا. فإن كانا ثقتين فعلى رأي جَمَاعَة لا يضر هَذَا الاختلاف؛ لأنه إن كَانَ الْحَدِيث عن هَذَا المعين فهو عدل، وإن
