الْحَدِيث. وهذا إنما يتجه إذا كَانَ لا دليل لنا عَلَى أَنَّ الْحَدِيث عَنْهُما جميعاً. أما إن دل دليل فلا اختلاف مثل أن يروي إنسان حديثاً عن رجل تارة، ويروي ذَلِكَ الْحَدِيث عن آخر تارة ثُمَّ يرويه عَنْهُمَا معاً في مرة ثالثة. وأما إن كَانَ أحد الراويين ضعيفاً فَقَدْ تردد الحال بَيْنَ أَن يَكُون عن القوي أَوْ عن الضعيف أَوْ عَنْهُمَا، وَهُوَ عَلَى أحد هَذِهِ التقديرات غَيْر حجة، ثُمَّ إِن هَذَا يشترط فِيْهِ أن لا يَكُون الطريقان مختلفين بَلْ يكونان عن رجل واحد. ومع ذَلِكَ فيجوز أن يَكُون رَوَاهُ عَنْهُمَا جميعاً. وَقَدْ أشار الحافظ السيوطي في " التدريب إلى بعض الاختلافات غير القادحة بصحة الْحَدِيث عِنْدَ الْمُحَدِّثِينَ، قَالَ : فمن ذَلِكَ أنهما أخرجا قصة جمل جابر من طرق، وفيها اختلاف كثير في مقدار الثمن، وفي اشتراط ركوبه. وَقَدْ رجّح البخاري الطرق الَّتِي فِيْهَا الاشتراط على غيرها مع تخريج الأمرين، ورجح أيضاً كون الثمن أوقية مع تخريجه ما يخالف ذَلِكَ». قلت والاختلاف في ثمن البعير أنه جاء بأوقية وفي رِوَايَة بأربعة دنانير، وَهُوَ يَكُون بأوقية على حساب الدينار بعشرة دراهم. وفي رِوَايَة أوقية ذهب، وفي رِوَايَة ومئتي درهم وفي رِوَايَة أربع أواقٍ، وفي رِوَايَة بعشرين ديناراً. وَقَدْ خرّجها البخاري جميعها ورجح أنه بأوقية، قال البخاري: وقول الشعبي بأوقية أكثر الاشتراط أكثر وأصح عندي. وَقَدْ فسّر الحافظ ابن حجر ذَلِكَ بقوله: «أي أكثر طرقاً وأصح مخرجاً، ثُمَّ قَالَ: «وما جنح إِلَيْهِ المصنف من ترجيح رِوَايَة الاشتراط هُوَ الجاري عَلَى طريقة المحققين من أهل الْحَدِيث لأنهم لا يتوقفون عن تصحيح الْحَدِيث إذا وقع فيه الاختلاف إلا إذا تكافأت الروايات، وَهُوَ شرط الاضطراب الَّذِي يرد بِهِ الخبر، وَهُوَ مفقود هنا مع إمكان الترجيح).
The introductory chapter: Statement of the nature of difference. The eighth topic: Defective difference and non-defective difference. There is no doubt that non-defective difference is not considered and has no effect according to hadith scholars, and when we intended to study these differences in chains and texts, we only meant the defective among them. The difference of narrators in a matter in which there is no contradiction is not harmful because it is a difference of variety, not a difference of contradiction. Sometimes the difference may be between two chains, one strong and the other weak, so such a difference does not defect because the correct narration is not defected by the weak narration nor affected. Likewise, there may be an apparent difference, but reconciliation between them is possible, such that the speaker may have expressed one meaning with the two wordings, so there is no problem. For example: in one version the narrator said 'from a man', and in the other version he named the man. This, and it is possible that the named person is the same as the unspecified one, so no contradiction. But if the narrator names a specific person in one narration and names another person in another narration, then this is a place of pause and scrutiny, because two matters conflict: one: it is possible that the hadith is from both men together. The second: that it is most likely that the narrator is one person about whom there is difference. So in this case, it is not free from either both men being trustworthy or not. If both are trustworthy, then in the view of a group this difference does not harm; because whether the hadith is from this specific one, he is reliable, or from the other, he is reliable, so however the hadith turns, it goes to a reliable person, so this difference does not harm. However, the experts among hadith scholars see that this is defective in the narration because it indicates the lack of precision (dabt) of its narrator. And precision is a condition for the authenticity of hadith. And this is only applicable if we have no evidence that the hadith is from both of them. But if evidence indicates, then there is no difference, such as a person narrating a hadith from one man sometimes, then narrating that same hadith from another sometimes, and then narrating it from both together a third time. And if one of the two narrators is weak, then the situation vacillates between being from the strong, or from the weak, or from both, and upon any of these estimates it is not a proof. Then, this requires that the two chains not be different but rather be from one man. Nevertheless, it is possible that he narrated it from both of them. And al-Hafiz al-Suyuti in al-Tadrib pointed to some non-defective differences regarding the authenticity of hadith according to hadith scholars. He said: 'Among that is that they both (Bukhari and Muslim) brought out the story of Jabir's camel from multiple chains, and in them there is much difference in the amount of the price and in the condition of riding it. Al-Bukhari preferred the chains that contain the condition over others while transmitting both matters, and also preferred that the price was an uqiya while transmitting what contradicts that.' I say: And the difference in the price of the camel is that it came as an uqiya, and in a narration as four dinars, and it is an uqiya according to the calculation of the dinar as ten dirhams, and in a narration an uqiya of gold, and in a narration two hundred dirhams, and in a narration four awaq, and in a narration twenty dinars. Al-Bukhari transmitted all of them and preferred that it was an uqiya. Al-Bukhari said: 'The statement of al-Sha'bi that it was an uqiya, the condition is more numerous and more authentic in my view.' And al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar interpreted that by saying: 'i.e., more numerous in chains and more authentic in source.' Then he said: 'And what the author inclined to of preferring the narration with the condition is the practice according to the method of the verifiers among hadith scholars, because they do not hesitate to authenticate a hadith when difference occurs in it unless the narrations are equally matched, which is the condition of disruption (idhtirab) by which the report is rejected, and it is lacking here with the possibility of preponderance.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the classification of differences (ikhtilaf) in hadith into two types: defective (qadih) and non-defective (ghayr qadih). The author opens by stating that non-defective differences are disregarded by hadith scholars and have no effect. The study is concerned only with defective differences. A difference that does not entail contradiction (tanāquḍ) is not harmful; it is a difference of variety (tanawwuʿ), not of contradiction (taḍād). Examples: a strong chain versus a weak chain—the weak does not impair the strong; or an apparent difference that can be reconciled, such as one narrator saying 'from a man' and another naming the same man. A problematic case arises when a narrator names different individuals in different narrations. Two possibilities exist: the hadith might be from both men, or the narrator is one person but there is confusion. If both are trustworthy, some scholars deem it harmless because either way the narrator is reliable. However, the leading hadith experts (jihābadha) consider this defective because it indicates a lack of precise memory (ḍabṭ), a condition for authenticity. If there is evidence that the hadith is from both (e.g., the narrator reports it from each separately and then from both together), then no problem. If one narrator is weak, the narration is not a proof. The passage then cites al-Suyuti's example from the story of Jabir's camel: multiple narrations differ on the price and on whether riding the camel was a condition. Al-Bukhari included all versions but preferred the one mentioning the condition and the price of an uqiya, because those narrations are more numerous and more authentic. Ibn Hajar explains that hadith scholars do not reject a hadith due to such differences as long as one narration can be preferred; true disruption (iḍṭirāb) requires equal strength of conflicting narrations, which is absent here. The author thus demonstrates that many differences in hadith are non-defective and do not affect the ruling derived from the hadith.

Key Terms
الاضطراب (al-iḍṭirāb): اختلاف في الروايات بحيث لا يمكن الجمع أو الترجيح، مع تكافؤ الروايات
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): تفضيل إحدى الروايتين على الأخرى بناءً على قوة السند أو المتن
الأوقية (al-ūqiyya): وحدة وزن تساوي أربعين درهماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Bukhari consider the differences in the price of Jabir's camel as non-defective?
A) Because all narrators were Companions.
B) Because he could reconcile the different numbers or prefer one version.
C) Because the hadith is about a camel sale and not about legal rulings.
D) Because he rejected all versions except one.

Key Takeaway
Differences in hadith are non-defective if reconciliation or preponderance (tarjīḥ) is possible, as demonstrated by al-Bukhari's handling of Jabir's camel.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Handling Ikhtilaf in Hadith: The Example of Jabir's Camel
Exposition of the principle that ikhtilaf in narrations does not impair authenticity if tarjih is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why disagreement (ikhtilaf) in hadith narrations does not necessarily impair authenticity, and apply the principle of tarjih to resolve conflicting reports.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf in hadith does not harm authenticity if tarjih is possible; only equal-strength conflict causes idtirab.

This passage from a usul al-fiqh text addresses a critical principle: disagreements among hadith narrations do not automatically weaken the hadith. Instead, scholars like al-Bukhari employ tarjih (preference) to resolve variations. The famous story of Jabir's camel illustrates this: multiple versions of the price and condition exist, yet the hadith remains authentic because Bukhari could prefer one set of narrations over others. Understanding this principle is vital for Hanafi usul, where conflicting evidence must be reconciled or preferred, not discarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Read the passage and find the main claim: that ikhtilaf (disagreement) in narrations does not harm authenticity if tarjih (preference) is possible. Note that this is a methodological rule in hadith criticism.
The passage cites al-Suyuti and Ibn Hajar to support this.
2
Analyze the example
Examine the story of Jabir's camel. List the varying details: the price (uqiyya, four dinars, 200 dirhams, etc.) and whether the ride was stipulated. Observe that Bukhari narrated all versions but preferred one.
Bukhari's preference was based on chain strength and the opinion of al-Sha'bi.
3
Understand tarjih
Tarjih is the process of preferring one narration over another due to factors like number of chains, reliability of narrators, or corroborating evidence. Here, Bukhari preferred the narrations with the stipulation and the price of an uqiyya.
Tarjih is a core tool in Hanafi usul for resolving conflicting proofs.
4
Distinguish from idtirab
Idtirab (disorder) occurs when conflicting narrations are equally strong, making preference impossible. Ibn Hajar clarifies that since tarjih was possible here, the hadith remains authentic. Only when narratives are exactly balanced does ikhtilaf become harmful.
This distinction is crucial for usul al-fiqh: not every conflict forces rejection.
5
Apply to usul al-fiqh
Reflect on how this principle applies to fiqh rulings. When two hadiths conflict on a legal issue, the Hanafi method is to attempt tarjih. If successful, the stronger narration is used for ruling; if not, further investigation or suspension may be required.
This principle underlies the Hanafi approach to ta'arud al-adillah (conflict of evidence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التمهيدي بيان ماهية الاختلاف عنهما جميعاً. وقد أشار الحافظ السيوطي في "التدريب" إلى بعض الاختلافات غير القادحة بصحة الحديث عند المحدثين، قال: فمن ذلك أنهما أخرجا قصة جمل جابر من طرق، وفيها اختلاف كثير في مقدار الثمن، وفي اشتراط ركوبه. وقد رجّح البخاري الطرق التي فيها الاشتراط على غيرها مع تخريج الأمرين، ورجح أيضاً كون الثمن أوقية مع تخريجه ما يخالف ذلك. قلت والاختلاف في ثمن البعير أنه جاء بأوقية وفي رواية بأربعة دنانير، وهو يكون بأوقية على حساب الدينار بعشرة دراهم. وفي رواية أوقية ذهب، وفي رواية ومئتي درهم وفي رواية أربع أواقٍ، وفي رواية بعشرين ديناراً. وقد خرّجها البخاري جميعها ورجح أنه بأوقية، قال البخاري: وقول الشعبي بأوقية أكثر الاشتراط أكثر وأصح عندي. وقد فسّر الحافظ ابن حجر ذلك بقوله: «أي أكثر طرقاً وأصح مخرجاً، ثم قال: «وما جنح إليه المصنف من ترجيح رواية الاشتراط هو الجاري على طريقة المحققين من أهل الحديث لأنهم لا يتوقفون عن تصحيح المتن إذا وقع فيه الاختلاف إلا إذا تكافأت الروايات، وهو شرط الاضطراب الذي يرد به الخبر، وهو مفقود هنا مع إمكان الترجيح).
The introductory chapter: Statement of the nature of disagreement between them both. Al-Hafiz al-Suyuti in 'al-Tadrib' pointed to some disagreements that do not impair the authenticity of hadith according to the muhaddithin. He said: Among that is that they (Bukhari and Muslim) narrated the story of Jabir's camel through multiple chains, and in them there is much disagreement about the amount of the price and about the stipulation of riding it. Al-Bukhari preferred the chains that contain the stipulation over others while still narrating both, and he also preferred that the price was an uqiyya while narrating what contradicts that. I say: The disagreement about the price of the camel is that it came as an uqiyya, and in one narration as four dinars, and it is an uqiyya by the rate of a dinar being ten dirhams. And in one narration an uqiyya of gold, and in one narration two hundred dirhams, and in one narration four uqiyyas, and in one narration twenty dinars. Al-Bukhari narrated all of them and preferred that it was an uqiyya. Al-Bukhari said: The saying of al-Sha'bi of an uqiyya, and the stipulation is more numerous and more authentic in my view. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar explained that by saying: 'I.e., more in terms of chains and more authentic in terms of source.' Then he said: 'What the author leaned towards of preferring the narration of the stipulation follows the method of the critical scholars of hadith, because they do not refrain from declaring a text authentic if disagreement occurs in it unless the narrations are equal, which is the condition of disorder (idtirab) that causes rejection of the report, and it is lacking here with the possibility of preference (tarjih).'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses a fundamental usul al-hadith principle that applies to usul al-fiqh: disagreement (ikhtilaf) in narrations does not necessarily impair the authenticity of a hadith, especially if a preference (tarjih) can be made. The author begins by citing al-Suyuti, who lists examples of non-harmful disagreements. The famous story of Jabir's camel, narrated by Bukhari and Muslim, contains variations in the price (e.g., an uqiyya, four dinars, two hundred dirhams) and whether the Prophet (peace be upon him) stipulated the camel's ride as a condition. Al-Bukhari, despite narrating all versions, preferred the narrations that include the stipulation and the greater likelihood of an uqiyya. He bases his preference on the strength of chains (kathrat al-turuq) and the opinion of al-Sha'bi. Ibn Hajar clarifies that such disagreement does not constitute idtirab (disorder), which would require equal strength of conflicting narrations. Since tarjih is possible, the hadith remains authentic. This principle is crucial in Hanafi usul, where conflicting evidence must be reconciled or preferred, not outright rejected.

Key Terms
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Disagreement or variation in the wording or content of hadith narrations.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): The act of preferring one narration over another based on external evidence (e.g., number of chains, reliability).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He rejected all narrations due to conflict.
B) He preferred the narration that mentioned a stipulation and an uqiyya.
C) He considered the hadith weak because of the differences.
D) He combined all narrations without preference.

Key Takeaway
Ikhtilaf in hadith narrations is not inherently harmful; scholars use tarjih to resolve disagreements, and only equal-strength conflict (idtirab) impairs authenticity.

Tadlis and Its Ruling in Hanafi Usul
Outlining tadlis sections and presenting differing rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tadlis and distinguish it from the stricter views of other scholars, applying the condition of explicit hearing.

Core Idea
Tadlis is concealment, not necessarily lying; Hanafis accept a mudallis's hadith if he states hearing explicitly.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of tadlis (deception in isnad) and the divergent opinions on its ruling. The source passage outlines the sections on tadlis and presents the extreme views of Shu'ba (equating tadlis with lying) and al-Bazzar (downplaying its severity). For Hanafi usul, the key is the narrator's explicitness: a mudallis is acceptable when he uses明确的 'sami'tu' (I heard) but rejected with vague terms like 'an' (from).

Guided Path
1
Define tadlis linguistically and technically
Tadlis means concealing a defect; in hadith, it refers to omitting a weak link in the chain or disguising a narrator's identity. The linguistic root is 'to conceal a flaw'.
Linguistically, it is not lying, but it may affect reliability.
2
Identify the two extreme scholarly positions
Some scholars, like Shu'ba, condemn tadlis as akin to lying ('brother of lying'), while others, like al-Bazzar, consider it merely beautifying the chain and not lying. These extremes frame the discourse.
Shu'ba's strong statement 'I would rather commit adultery than tadlis' shows severity.
3
Locate the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school adopts a middle path: tadlis is not inherently invalidating. If the mudallis narrator is trustworthy and explicitly states hearing (e.g., 'sami'tu'), his narration is accepted. If he uses vague terms ('an', 'qala'), it is rejected.
This aligns with the principle that certainty is required for acceptance.
4
Apply to a sample hadith
Consider a hadith narrated by a mudallis: if he says 'sami'tu', accept; if he says 'an', do not accept unless corroborated. This determines the ruling of the hadith in Hanafi fiqh.
This application shows the practical effect of the usul rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second: The ruling of tadlis and the ruling of one known for it: It has passed in the introductory chapter in defining tadlis linguistically that the sum of its meanings amount to concealing a defect, and lying is not among its meanings. Despite that, the scholars differed regarding its ruling and the ruling of its people. Some of them, among them Shu'ba, were strict about it, and it is narrated from him that he said: 'Tadlis is the brother of lying.' Commentary
This passage begins by announcing that the author will discuss the differences in chains in two sections, the first of which is on the effect of tadlis (deception in reporting) on hadith differences. He recalls that the linguistic definition of tadlis was given in the introductory chapter and postpones the technical definition. He then lists the subsections of this section: (1) types of tadlis, (2) ruling of tadlis and ruling of one known for it, (3) ruling of the mudallas hadith, and (4) the effect of tadlis on differences among narrators and its effect on juristic differences. The author then explains that the types have already been discussed in the introductory chapter. Then he begins the section on ruling: he states that the linguistic meanings of tadlis all point to concealing a defect, not lying. Despite that, scholars differed. Some, like Shu'ba, were very harsh, considering tadlis a brother of lying, and even said committing adultery is preferable to tadlis. Others, like Abu Bakr al-Bazzar, were lenient, saying tadlis is not lying but merely beautifying the apparent chain. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the legal impact of tadlis.

المدلس (al-mudallis): A narrator known for committing tadlis

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the hadith of a mudallis if he explicitly states hearing; tadlis alone does not invalidate a narration.

Three Madhhabs on Catching Jumu'ah: The Usul Impact of Hadith Discrepancy
Analyzing three madhhab positions on when one catches Jumu'ah based on differing hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a variant in hadith transmission (sanad/matn) leads to three distinct rulings on catching Jumu'ah, and evaluate the Hanafi position using usul principles.

Core Idea
Hadith variants with different wording produce differences in the fiqh of Jumu'ah catching.

In this lesson, we explore a classic usul al-fiqh case: how a single hadith, transmitted with an error in chain and wording, becomes the source of three madhhab positions on when one has 'caught' the Friday prayer. The Hanafi school's view that catching any part of the prayer suffices is contrasted with the majority's requirement to catch a rak'ah, and a minority that requires the sermon. You will learn to trace each opinion back to its hadith evidence and identify the usul principle at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith variant
In the text, Ibn Abi Hatim critiques a hadith that includes 'of the Friday prayer'. Note the correct version without it.
The error is in both text and chain – this is key to the dispute.
2
Map the three madhhab positions
List the first (sermon required), second (any part suffices), third (rak'ah required).
3
Trace each group's proof
For each, what dalil do they use? Note the first uses ijma, the second uses the hadith and concept of congregation, the third uses the corrected hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does Abu Hanifa justify catching any part? What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)?
5
Evaluate the strength of each position
Which opinion aligns best with the corrected hadith? The author notes the first contradicts explicit Sunnah.

الفصل الأول الاختلاف في السند
وَقَالَ ابن أبي حاتم أيضاً: سألت أبي عن حَدِيث رَوَاهُ بقية، عن يونس، عـن الزهري عن سالم عن ابن عمر عن النَّبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «من أدرك ركعة من صلاة الجمعة وغيرها فَقَدْ أدرك». قَالَ أبي هَذَا خطأ الْمَثْن والإسناد إنما هُوَ: الزهري، عن أبي سلمة، عن أبي هُرَيْرَة ، عن النبي ، من أدرك من صلاة ركعة فقد أدركها»، وأما قوله: «من صلاة الجمعة فليس هَذَا فِي الْحَدِيث، فوهم في كليهما». أثر هذا الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء المقدار الذي تدرك به صلاة الجمعة) : اختلف الفقهاء في حكم من سبق في صلاة الجمعة على ثلاثة مذاهب: الأول: لا تصح الجمعة لِمَنْ لَمْ يدرك شيئاً من خطبة الإمام. وبه قال الهادوية مـن الزيدية. وروي عن عمر بن بن الخطاب)، ومجاهد، وعطاء)، وطاووس، ومكحول . وحجتهم أن الإجماع منعقد عَلَى أن الإمام لَوْ لَمْ يخطب بالناس لَمْ يُصلوا إلا أربعاً، فدل ذلِكَ عَلَى أن الخطبة جزء من الصلاة). وهذا الرأي مخالف لصريح السنة كما يأتي.
الثاني: من أدرك الإمام يوم الجمعة في أي جزء من صلاته صلى مَعَهُ ما أدرك وأكمل الجمعة فإنه أدركها، حَتَّى وإن أدركه في التشهد أو سجود السهو. وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيْفَة وأبو يوسف القاضي. واستدلوا بأن صلاة الجمعة ركعتان بجماعة، ومن أدرك الإمام قَبْلَ سلامه فَقَدْ أدرك الجماعة، غاية ما هناك أنه مسبوق، والمسبوق يصلي مع الإمام ما أدرك ثُمَّ يتم ما فاته، وما فاته هنا ركعتان لا أربع، فلا يجب عَلَيْهِ أن يصلي أكثر مما أحرم ناوياً صلاته).
الثالث: ذهب أكثر أهل العلم وجمهور الفقهاء إلى أن من أدرك الركعة الثانية مع الإمام فقد أدرك الجمعة، وعليه أن يأتي بركعة أخرى بَعْدَ فراغ الإمام، فإن لَمْ يدرك مِنْهَا ركعة، وذلك بأن أدرك الإمام بَعْدَ أن رفع رأسه من ركوع الركعة الثانية، فإنه يأتي بَعْدَ فراغ الإمام بأربع ركعات ظهراً؛ لأنَّهُ لَمْ يدرك الجمعة أصلا. وهذا القَوْل مروي عن ابن مسعود، وابن عمر وأنس، وسعيد.
Chapter One: Disagreement in the Chain of Narration. Ibn Abi Hatim also said: I asked my father about a hadith narrated by Baqiyyah, from Yunus, from al-Zuhri, from Salim, from Ibn Umar, from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Whoever catches a rak'ah of the Friday prayer and others has caught it.' My father said: This is an error in the text and chain; rather it is: al-Zuhri, from Abu Salamah, from Abu Hurayrah, from the Prophet: 'Whoever catches a rak'ah of the prayer has caught it.' As for his saying 'of the Friday prayer', this is not in the hadith; he was mistaken in both. The effect of this hadith on the disagreement of the jurists regarding the amount by which the Friday prayer is caught: The jurists disagreed on the ruling of one who precedes in the Friday prayer into three madhhabs: First: The Friday prayer is not valid for one who does not catch anything of the imam's sermon. This was held by the Hadawiyyah of the Zaydiyyah, and narrated from Umar ibn al-Khattab, Mujahid, 'Ata', Tawus, and Makhul. Their proof is that there is consensus that if the imam did not deliver the sermon to the people, they would pray only four rak'ahs. This indicates that the sermon is a part of the prayer. This opinion contradicts the explicit Sunnah as will come. Second: Whoever catches the imam on Friday in any part of his prayer prays with him what he catches and completes the Friday prayer; he has caught it, even if he catches him in the tashahhud or prostrations of forgetfulness. This was the view of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf al-Qadi. They argued that the Friday prayer is two rak'ahs with congregation, and whoever catches the imam before his salutation has caught the congregation. At most he is a latecomer, and the latecomer prays with the imam what he catches and then completes what he missed. What he misses here is two rak'ahs, not four, so he does not have to pray more than what he intended in his prayer. Third: The majority of scholars and the generality of jurists held that whoever catches the second rak'ah with the imam has caught the Friday prayer, and he must perform another rak'ah after the imam finishes. If he does not catch a rak'ah of it, that is by catching the imam after he raises his head from the bowing of the second rak'ah, then he prays four rak'ahs of Zuhr after the imam finishes, because he has not caught the Friday prayer at all. This opinion is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, Anas, and Sa'id.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute showing how a difference in the transmission (sanad and matn) of a hadith leads to three distinct rulings among jurists regarding what constitutes catching the Friday prayer. The author begins by citing Ibn Abi Hatim's criticism of a hadith that mistakenly attributes to the Prophet the phrase 'of the Friday prayer' in addition to 'a rak'ah'. The correct version, according to Ibn Abi Hatim's father, is general: 'Whoever catches a rak'ah of prayer has caught it.' This textual error in the chain and wording becomes the basis for disagreement. The first madhhab (Zaydi and early figures) requires attending the sermon, arguing that consensus shows the sermon is part of Jumu'ah, so missing it means one must pray four rak'ahs. The second madhhab (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf) holds that catching any part of the prayer—even the tashahhud—is sufficient, because Jumu'ah is a two-rak'ah congregational prayer; catching the imam before taslim means one catches the congregation, and the latecomer completes only two rak'ahs. The third madhhab (majority) distinguishes between catching a rak'ah (then one completes Jumu'ah with an extra rak'ah) and not catching a rak'ah (then one prays four rak'ahs of Zuhr). The Hanafi position (second) is highlighted as based on a broad understanding of congregation. The author notes that the first opinion contradicts explicit sunnah, implying preference for the latter two.

Key Terms
الخطبة (al-khutbah): The sermon delivered before the Friday prayer, considered by some as an integral part of Jumu'ah.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): A latecomer who misses part of the congregational prayer and must complete what he missed after the imam's taslim.
الركعة (al-rak'ah): A unit of prayer consisting of bowing and two prostrations. Catching the rak'ah requires catching the imam in the bowing position (according to the majority).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a person catches the Imam in the tashahhud of the Friday prayer, what should he do?
A) Pray four rak'ahs of Zuhr because he missed the Friday prayer.
B) Pray with the imam and then complete two rak'ahs for Jumu'ah.
C) Leave the mosque and pray alone.
D) Pray with the imam and then complete four rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
Three madhhab positions on catching Jumu'ah arise from a hadith variant: the Hanafi school emphasizes congregation, the majority requires a rak'ah, and a minority requires the sermon.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Disagreement on Catching Jumu'ah: Hanafi vs. Majority
Presents two opinions on catching Jumu'ah and lists supporters of majority view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi and majority views on catching Jumu'ah and identify the specific textual basis for each position.

Core Idea
For Jumu'ah, Hanafis consider catching any part enough; the majority require at least one rak'ah.

This passage illustrates how different interpretations of the same prophetic practice lead to distinct fiqh rulings. The Hanafi position emphasizes the congregational nature of Jumu'ah, while the majority rely on a hadith about catching a rak'ah. Understanding this difference clarifies key usul principles about textual authority and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State that catching any part of the Friday prayer with the imam, even tashahhud or sajdat al-sahw, suffices to have caught Jumu'ah.
This is held by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
2
Identify the majority position
State that catching a full rak'ah is required; if one catches less, he has not caught Jumu'ah and must pray Zuhr.
List of supporters includes many Companions and early scholars.
3
Analyze the evidence for each
Hanafi dalil: Jumu'ah is two rak'ahs with congregation; catching any part means catching the congregation. Majority dalil: hadith 'Whoever catches a rak'ah has caught the prayer'.
Hanafis argue the hadith is general and doesn't override the specific nature of Jumu'ah.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If someone catches the imam in tashahhud on Friday, Hanafi says he prays two rak'ahs Jumu'ah; majority says he prays four rak'ahs Zuhr.
This difference directly affects weekly worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الاختلاف في السند
الثاني: من أدرك الإمام يوم الجمعة في أي جزء من صلاته صلى معه ما أدرك وأكمل الجمعة فإنه أدركها، حتى وإن أدركه في التشهد أو سجود السهو. وإليه ذهب أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف القاضي. واستدلوا بأن صلاة الجمعة ركعتان بجماعة، ومن أدرك الإمام قبل سلامه فقد أدرك الجماعة، غاية ما هناك أنه مسبوق، والمسبوق يصلي مع الإمام ما أدرك ثم يتم ما فاته، وما فاته هنا ركعتان لا أربع، فلا يجب عليه أن يصلي أكثر مما أحرم ناوياً صلاته.
الثالث: ذهب أكثر أهل العلم وجمهور الفقهاء إلى أن من أدرك الركعة الثانية مع الإمام فقد أدرك الجمعة، وعليه أن يأتي بركعة أخرى بعد فراغ الإمام، فإن لم يدرك منها ركعة، وذلك بأن أدرك الإمام بعد أن رفع رأسه من ركوع الركعة الثانية، فإنه يأتي بعد فراغ الإمام بأربع ركعات ظهراً؛ لأنه لم يدرك الجمعة أصلا. وهذا القول مروي عن ابن مسعود، وابن عمر، وأنس، وسعيد بن المسيب، والأسود بن يزيد، والحسن البصري، وعروة، والنخعي -في إحدى الروايتين عنه-، والزهري، ومالك، والأوزاعي، والثوري، وإسحاق، وأبي ثور، وأحمد، وزفر.
Chapter One: Disagreement in the Chain of Transmission. Second: Whoever catches up with the imam on Friday in any part of his prayer, he prays with him what he catches and completes the Friday prayer, and he has caught it, even if he catches him in the tashahhud or the prostration of forgetfulness. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf al-Qadi hold this view. They argued that the Friday prayer is two rak'ahs with congregation, and whoever catches the imam before his salutation has caught the congregation; at most he is a latecomer, and the latecomer prays with the imam what he catches and then completes what he missed, and what he missed here is two rak'ahs, not four, so he is not required to pray more than what he intended when he entered the prayer. Third: The majority of scholars and the general body of jurists hold that whoever catches the second rak'ah with the imam has caught the Friday prayer, and he must perform another rak'ah after the imam finishes. If he does not catch a rak'ah from it—that is, if he catches the imam after he has raised his head from the bowing of the second rak'ah—then he performs four rak'ahs of Zuhr after the imam finishes, because he has not caught the Friday prayer at all. This opinion is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, Anas, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, al-Aswad ibn Yazid, al-Hasan al-Basri, 'Urwa, al-Nakha'i (in one of two narrations from him), al-Zuhri, Malik, al-Awza'i, al-Thawri, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, Ahmad, and Zufar.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an example of how differences in the understanding of hadith or sunnah lead to differing fiqh rulings. The text presents two positions on who catches the Friday (Jumu'ah) prayer. The first position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, is based on the principle that the Friday prayer is two rak'ahs performed in congregation. Therefore, catching any part of the prayer with the imam, even the final tashahhud, means one has caught the Jumu'ah. The latecomer completes his prayer as two rak'ahs because he intended the Friday prayer. The second position, held by the majority, distinguishes between catching a full rak'ah and less than a rak'ah. If one catches the second rak'ah (i.e., at least one rak'ah with the imam), he has caught Jumu'ah and prays one rak'ah after the imam. If he catches only after the ruku' of the second rak'ah, he has not caught the Jumu'ah and must pray four rak'ahs of Zuhr. The Hanafi ruling deems catching any part sufficient, while the majority require at least one rak'ah. This difference may stem from different interpretations of the hadith 'Whoever catches a rak'ah of the prayer has caught the prayer' or similar texts. The passage then lists numerous scholars who support the majority view, including several Companions and later jurists.

Key Terms
مسبوق (masbuq): A latecomer who joins the congregation after the imam has begun the prayer.
أدرك (adraka): To catch up with the imam in any part of the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He has caught Jumu'ah and prays one more rak'ah, making it two rak'ahs total.
B) He has caught Jumu'ah and completes two rak'ahs with the imam.
C) He has not caught Jumu'ah and must pray four rak'ahs of Zuhr.
D) He has caught Jumu'ah and prays two rak'ahs after the imam finishes.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement on catching Jumu'ah exemplifies how usul principles (interpretation of evidence) lead to multiple valid fiqh positions.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Impact of Hadith Variants (Ziyada) on Fiqh Rulings
Lists scholars and their ruling based on a hadith variant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a variant hadith wording (ziyada) can lead to differences in fiqh rulings among scholars, and evaluate the authenticity of such variants using sanad analysis.

Core Idea
A single hadith with a variant wording can split scholars; the authenticity of the ziyada determines its legal weight.

This passage lists a group of early scholars who held a specific ruling on whether a person who misses a rak'ah in Friday prayer should add another rak'ah. Their evidence was a variant wording in a hadith about Friday prayer. However, that variant was later deemed inauthentic. This lesson examines how such textual differences — even within a single hadith — can generate legal disagreement (ikhtilaf). You will learn to parse a classical usul text that demonstrates the interplay between sanad, matn, and fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key elements
Locate the list of scholars, the ruling quoted from Ahmad, and the hadith variant referenced. Note that the passage presents a group of early authorities who agreed on a specific fiqh position.
The passage does not name the opposing view, but the ikhtilaf is implied by the mention of the variant's inauthenticity.
2
Classify the textual difference
Recognise that the hadith variant contains a ziyada (additional wording). In Hanafi usul, a ziyada from a reliable narrator is accepted, but the authenticity of this particular ziyada was questioned.
The text states that the ziyada was found inauthentic, which affects its evidentiary value.
3
Assess the authenticity claim
The passage explicitly says the addition is not authentic. This implies that the hadith variant's sanad was weak or that the addition was not preserved in reliable transmissions.
Even though the ziyada is not authentic, the passage notes that the scholars had other detailed proofs, so their ruling may still be supported.
4
Relate to ikhtilaf al-fuqaha
The difference in authenticity assessment of the same hadith variant is a classical cause of ikhtilaf. Scholars who accepted the variant ruled one way; those who rejected it ruled another.
This shows that ikhtilaf often arises from hadith criticism rather than from different primary sources.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul principles
Hanafi usul gives significant weight to the sanad. A ziyada from a reliable narrator is ordinarily accepted, but when the ziyada is found to be inauthentic, it is not used as evidence. The passage exemplifies the Hanafi method of weighing hadith variants.
The Hanafi school's acceptance of ziyada is conditional on the reliability of its chain.

الفصل الأول: الاختلاف في السند
المسيب، والأسود بن يزيد، والحسن البصري، وعروة، والنخعي - في إحدى الروايتين عنه -، والزهري، ومالك، والأوزاعي، والثوري، وإسحاق، وأبي ثور، وأحمد، وزفر، ابن الهذيل، ومحمد بن الحسن.
قال أحمد: «إذا فاته الركوع صلى أربعاً، وإذا ترك ركعة صلى إليها أخرى».
واستدلوا على هذا بما ورد في بعض طرق هذا الحديث: من صلاة الجمعة، وقد تبين عدم صحة هذه الزيادة فيما مضى، على أن لهم أدلة تفصيلية أخرى سوى هذا ترجّح ما ذهبوا إليه.
Chapter One: Difference in the Chain. Al-Musayyib, al-Aswad ibn Yazid, al-Hasan al-Basri, 'Urwah, al-Nakha'i - in one of two narrations from him - al-Zuhri, Malik, al-Awza'i, al-Thawri, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, Ahmad, Zufar, Ibn al-Hudhayl, and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. Ahmad said: 'If one misses the bowing, he prays four; and if he leaves a rak'ah, he prays another one to it.' They argued for this based on what is mentioned in some versions of this hadith: concerning Friday prayer, and it has been shown that this addition is not authentic in what preceded, although they have other detailed proofs besides this that support their view.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a list of early scholars who held a specific ruling regarding the prayer of someone who misses the bowing (ruku') or leaves a rak'ah. The ruling, as quoted from Imam Ahmad, is that such a person prays four rak'ahs (presumably the Friday prayer) and if he misses a whole rak'ah, he adds another rak'ah. The passage explains that their evidence is a variant of a hadith about Friday prayer that includes an additional wording (ziyada). However, the author states that this addition has been shown to be inauthentic. Nevertheless, the scholars have other detailed proofs for their position. The passage highlights how differences in the chain (sanad) and text (matn) of a hadith can lead to different fiqh rulings among scholars.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyada): An additional wording in some versions of a hadith that is not found in others.
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.
متن (matn): The text of the hadith itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Differences in hadith transmission, particularly additions (ziyada), can lead to divergent fiqh rulings, and the authenticity of such additions must be verified through sanad criticism.

Juristic Dispute on Looking at Spouse's Private Parts
Presenting two main juristic opinions on looking at spouse's genitals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two juristic opinions on looking at spouse's private parts and explain the Hanafi school's position of permissibility with preference to avoid.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits spouses to look at each other's private parts, but recommends avoiding it to uphold modesty.

This dispute highlights how different usul principles and interpretations of textual evidence can lead to divergent rulings even on intimate matters. The passage presents two main views, and as a parsing exercise, we will break down each position and the Hanafi nuance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
List the opposed rulings (makruh vs ibahah) and the schools associated with each.
First opinion: disliked (makruh) – Shafi'i and Hanbali. Second opinion: permissible (ibahah) – Hanafi, Maliki, Zahiri.
2
Note the Hanafi nuance
The Hanafis permit but say it is better not to look (al-awla 'adam al-nazar).
This distinguishes their position from a plain permissibility.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect on modesty practices and marital relations.
Different schools may advise different behaviors during intimacy.
4
Connect to usul
Reflect on what usul principles might underlie the difference (e.g., original permissibility vs. blocking means).
This helps understand how usul al-fiqh generates fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أثر الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء نظر الزوج إلى فرج زوجته أو حليلته) : اختلف الفقهاء في جواز نظر الزوج إلى فرج زوجته أو ملك يده على مذهبين: الأول: يكره للزوج النظر إلى فرج زوجته كما يكره للزوجة النظر إلى فرج زوجها، وإليه ذهب الشافعية)، والحنابلة). الثاني: ذهب جمهور الفقهاء إلى إباحة نظر كُلّ من الزوجين إلى فرج الآخر، ونظر المالك إلى فرج مملوكته، ونظر المملوكة إلى فرج مالكها. وبه قَالَ الحنفية ()، والمالكية ) ، والظاهرية ). ومع ذلك فإن الحنفية قالوا: الأولى عدم النظر .
Effect of the hadith on the disagreement of jurists: Looking of the husband at the private part of his wife or his slave-woman. The jurists differed on the permissibility of a husband looking at his wife's private part or his slave-woman's into two schools: First: It is disliked for the husband to look at his wife's private part, just as it is disliked for the wife to look at her husband's private part. This is the view of the Shafi'is and Hanbalis. Second: The majority of jurists held the permissibility of each spouse looking at the other's private part, and the owner looking at his female slave's private part, and the female slave looking at her owner's private part. This is the view of the Hanafis, Malikis, and Zahiris. Despite that, the Hanafis said: 'It is better not to look.'
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a juristic dispute (ikhtilaf) on the ruling of spouses looking at each other's private parts. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools consider it disliked (makruh), while the majority, including the Hanafi, Maliki, and Zahiri schools, permit it (ibahah). However, the Hanafis add that it is preferable to avoid. This difference may stem from varying interpretations of modesty and relevant hadiths. The passage illustrates how hadith differences affect fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibahah): Permissibility, a legal ruling indicating no prohibition
كراهة (karahah): Disliked, a ruling of disapproval without strict prohibition
الأولى عدم النظر (al-awla 'adam al-nazar): It is better not to look; a phrase indicating preference despite permissibility

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Disliked
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits looking at a spouse's private parts but recommends avoiding it for modesty, illustrating how a ruling can be permissible yet discouraged.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Sanad Discrepancy and Its Impact on Hadith Reliability
Analyzing chain differences that led to conflicting narrations of a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify chain-related defects (tadlis, lack of direct hearing) in a hadith and evaluate their impact on textual reliability according to Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
A single tadlis in the chain can render a hadith 'ghayr mahfuz' and shift reliance to the sounder narration from the majority.

This passage is a detailed textual analysis of how an isnad discrepancy—specifically the tadlis of Ibn Jurayj—led to conflicting versions of the hadith regarding the Prophet's ring. By examining the critics' statements and comparing the two transmissions, we will see how careful sanad analysis determines which version is reliable and how this affects the legal ruling derived from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed hadith and the alleged defect
The hadith concerns the Prophet's silver ring with the inscription 'Muhammad Rasul Allah'. Al-Nasa'i declared the version via Ibn Jurayj as 'ghayr mahfuz' (not preserved), pinpointing the defect to Ibn Jurayj's tadlis.
Tadlis here means Ibn Jurayj's ambiguous wording that hides a lack of direct hearing from al-Zuhri.
2
Examine the critics' statements about Ibn Jurayj's link to al-Zuhri
Abu Zur'ah, Yahya al-Qattan, and Ibn Ma'in all confirm that Ibn Jurayj did not hear directly from al-Zuhri; he only received a written juz'. This weakens the chain.
Hanafi usul considers direct hearing (samā') as a minimal condition for accepting a hadith unless other factors allow mursal.
3
Compare the two versions of the ring hadith
The sound version via Thabit and 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Suhayb from Anas is narrated by many compilers (Bukhari, Muslim, etc.) and says the Prophet forbade copying his engraving. The defective version via al-Zuhri is from a minority route.
The textual difference is significant: one version forbids copying the exact inscription, the other is ambiguous.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle that a defective chain cannot establish a ruling
Since the chain via Ibn Jurayj is weak due to tadlis and lack of direct hearing, the hadith is not reliable enough to derive a legal ruling. The sound version (via Thabit) is preferred and becomes the basis for the rule.
In Hanafi usul, a hadith with a weak chain (like ghayr mahfuz) is not used as proof unless supported by stronger evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الاختلاف في السند

سعد، وصرّح بِهِ لغيرهم كَمَا أنه - وعند تحديثه لأهل البصرة - لَمْ يَكُنْ متقناً لحفظ الْمَتْن فأخطأ فِيْهِ، لذا قَالَ النسائي عقب تخريجه: «هَذَا حَدِيث غَيْر محفوظ». فانحصر الخطأ في تدليس ابن جريج، ولهذا نجد الحافظ ابن حجر يقول: «ولا علة لَهُ عندي إلا تدليس ابن جريج، فإن وجد عَنْهُ التصريح بالسماع فلا مانع من الحكم بصحته في نقدي». ومما يزيدنا يقيناً بكون الخطأ في هَذَا الْحَدِيث من ابن جريج: أن أكثر الحفاظ عَلَى تضعيف روايته عن الزهري مطلقاً، فَقَالَ أبو زرعة الرازي: «أخبرني بعض أصحابنا، عن قريش بن أنس، عن ابن جريج، قَالَ : ما سَمِعْتُ من الزهري شيئاً، إنما أعطاني الزهري جزءاً فكتبته وأجازه». وَقَالَ يحيى بن سعيد القطان: «كَانَ ابن جريج يصحح أنه سَمِعَ من الزهري شَيْئاً - يعني الفلاس - فجهدت بِهِ في حَدِيث إن ناساً من اليهود غزوا مع رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فأسهم لَهُمْ، فَلَمْ يصحح أنه سَمِعَ من الزهري». وَقَالَ ابن معين: «ليس بشيء في الزهري». ونقل ابن محرز عن ابن معين أنه قَالَ: «كَانَ يحيى بن سعيد لا يوثقه في الزهري». ومما تجدر الإشارة إِلَيْهِ أن أكثر الحفاظ يرون أن الزهري نفسه أخطأ في هَذَا الْحَدِيث، إذ خالف جمهور الرُّوَاة عن أنس في لفظ الْحَدِيث عَلَى النحو الآتي: رَوَاهُ ثابت عن أنس بن مالك: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم صنع خاتماً من ورق، فنقش فِيْهِ: مُحَمَّد رَسُول الله، ثُمَّ قَالَ: لا تنقشوا عَلَيْهِ. الْحَدِيث أخرجه عبد الرزاق، وأحمد، والترمذي، وأبو الشيخ، والبيهقي، والبغوي. ورواه عبد العزيز بن صهيب عن أنس بن مالك: أن رَسُول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اتخذ خاتماً من فضة ونقش فِيْهِ: مُحَمَّد رَسُول الله، وَقَالَ: إِني اتخذت خاتماً من ورق ونقشت فيه: مُحَمَّد رَسُول الله فلا ينقشن أحد عَلَى نقشه. أخرجه ابن سعد، وابن أبي شيبة، وأحمد، والبخاري، ومسلم، وابن ماجه، والنسائي، وأبو يعلى، وأبو عوانة، وابن حبان، وأبو الشيخ، وأبو نعيم.
Chapter One: Difference in the Chain. Sa'd, and he stated it for others as well - and when he narrated to the people of Basra - he was not proficient in memorizing the text and erred in it, hence al-Nasa'i said after his extraction: 'This hadith is not preserved.' So the error was confined to the tadlis of Ibn Jurayj, and for this we find al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar saying: 'There is no defect in it in my view except the tadlis of Ibn Jurayj; if explicit hearing is found from him, there is no objection to judging it as authentic in my criticism.' And what increases our certainty that the error in this hadith is from Ibn Jurayj is that most hadith critics weaken his narration from al-Zuhri absolutely. Abu Zur'ah al-Razi said: 'Some of our companions told me, from Quraysh ibn Anas, from Ibn Jurayj, who said: I did not hear anything from al-Zuhri; al-Zuhri only gave me a part (juz') and I wrote it and he authorized it.' And Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: 'Ibn Jurayj claimed that he heard something from al-Zuhri' - meaning al-Fallas - 'so I exerted myself regarding a hadith that some Jews raided with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he gave them a share, but he did not confirm that he heard from al-Zuhri.' And Ibn Ma'in said: 'He is nothing in al-Zuhri.' And Ibn Muhriz narrated from Ibn Ma'in that he said: 'Yahya ibn Sa'id did not consider him reliable in al-Zuhri.' And it is worth noting that most hadith critics believe that al-Zuhri himself erred in this hadith, for he contradicted the majority of narrators from Anas in the wording of the hadith as follows: Thabit narrated from Anas ibn Malik that the Prophet, peace be upon him, made a ring of silver and engraved on it: Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, then said: 'Do not engrave upon it.' The hadith was reported by 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, Abu al-Shaykh, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Baghawi. And 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Suhayb narrated from Anas ibn Malik that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, took a silver ring and engraved on it: Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, and said: 'I have taken a silver ring and engraved on it: Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, so let no one engrave upon his engraving.' It was reported by Ibn Sa'd, Ibn Abi Shaybah, Ahmad, al-Bukhari, Muslim, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i, Abu Ya'la, Abu 'Awanah, Ibn Hibban, Abu al-Shaykh, and Abu Nu'aym.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of a hadith discrepancy arising from chain (sanad) differences. The author begins by identifying the hadith in question: the Prophet's ring inscription. Al-Nasa'i declared it 'ghayr mahfuz' (not preserved), pinpointing the error to Ibn Jurayj's tadlis (concealing a weak link). Ibn Hajar confirms that the only defect is tadlis, suggesting that if explicit samā' (hearing) were confirmed, the hadith would be sound. The author then strengthens this by citing critics who universally weaken Ibn Jurayj's narrations from al-Zuhri: Abu Zur'ah mentions Ibn Jurayj's admission that he did not hear directly from al-Zuhri but only wrote from a juz'; Yahya al-Qattan recounts his failed attempt to verify Ibn Jurayj's hearing; and Ibn Ma'in dismisses Ibn Jurayj as insignificant in al-Zuhri. Finally, the author notes that even al-Zuhri himself may have erred: the sound version through Thabit differs from the one through 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Suhayb. Thabit's version has the Prophet forbidding engraving the same inscription, while 'Abd al-'Aziz's version has the Prophet forbidding others from copying his ring. The author lists the many compilers who reported the sound versions, emphasizing their widespread acceptance. The lesson underscores how careful sanad analysis resolves contradiction and determines the reliable text.

Key Terms
إسناد (isnād): The chain of narrators through which a hadith is transmitted.
تدليس (tadlīs): Concealing a weak link in the chain by using ambiguous wording that suggests direct hearing.
غير محفوظ (ghayr maḥfūẓ): A term used by critics meaning the hadith is not preserved (i.e., it is defective in memorization or chain).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary defect in the chain of the ring hadith that led to it being declared 'ghayr mahfuz'?
A) The narrator Thabit was unreliable.
B) Ibn Jurayj was known for tadlis and did not hear directly from al-Zuhri.
C) The hadith contradicts a verse of the Quran.
D) The hadith has a missing link (mursal) in the chain.

Key Takeaway
A single identified tadlis in a chain can render a hadith unreliable, and the sound version from the majority must be preferred for deriving legal rulings.

Ruling on Wearing Silver Rings: Hanafi Usul Analysis
Dispute analysis of men's silver ring ruling across madhahib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on wearing silver rings, distinguish the conditions (intention and weight) that affect the ruling, and explain how differing interpretations of prophetic actions lead to juristic disagreement.

Core Idea
Intention transforms the ruling: wearing a silver ring is permissible but disliked if worn for pride.

The Prophetic practice of wearing and discarding rings is interpreted differently by scholars, leading to a spectrum of rulings. In this lesson, we explore how the Hanafi school balances permissibility with caution, emphasizing intention and avoidance of extravagance. Understanding this dispute illuminates the role of usul in deriving nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prophetic Action and Its Interpretations
The Prophet wore a silver ring for sealing, then discarded it along with others' engraved rings to avoid confusion. This action is interpreted in multiple ways.
Ibn Hajar's fourth interpretation is highlighted.
2
Locate the Hanafi Position
Hanafis rule that wearing a silver ring is permissible (mubah) if free from pride; disliked (makruh) if intended for arrogance or self-exaltation. They recommend the ring weigh less than one mithqal to avoid extravagance.
3
Compare with Other Madhahib
The majority (including Shafi'is) permit absolutely; some Shafi'is forbid if weight exceeds one mithqal; Malikis recommend it for following Sunnah but forbid if it leads to vanity.
This shows the spectrum of rulings from the same source.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Proof Structure
The Hanafi usul principle: prophetic actions (fi'l) indicate permissibility unless there is evidence of prohibition. The Prophet's wearing a silver ring establishes its permissibility. The discarding of the ring due to social imitation shows that context matters.
Intention (niyyah) becomes a key factor in the ruling.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
A Hanafi may wear a silver ring without sin if no pride, while a stricter Shafi'i may consider it forbidden if it exceeds a mithqal. The practical difference lies in the permissibility conditioned on intention and weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول الاختلاف في السند
وذهب ابن حجر إلى تأويل رابع: هُوَ أنه اتخذ خاتم الذهب للزينة وتتابع الناس فِيْهِ، فوقع تحريمه فطرحه، وَقَالَ: «لا ألبسه أبداً»، فطرح الناس خواتيمهم تبعاً لَهُ، ثُمَّ احتاج إلى الخاتم لأجل الختم، فاتخذه من فضة ونقش فيه اسمه الكريم، فتبعه الناس أَيْضاً عَلَى تِلْكَ الخواتيم المنقوشة، فرمى بِهِ حَتَّى رمى الناس تِلْكَ الخواتيم المنقوشة حَتَّى لا تفوته مصلحة نقش اسمه بوقوع الاشتراك، فلما عدمت خواتيمهم جميعاً رجع إلى خاتمه الخاص به فصار يتختم به.
أثر الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء حكم لبس خاتم الفضة للرجال: اختلف الفقهاء في حكم التختم بالفضة للرجال على النحو الآتي:
.١. ذهب جمهور العلماء من المتقدمين والمتأخرين إلى جواز اتخاذ خاتم الفضة، سواء كانَ ذا سلطان أم غيره. وبه قَالَ الشافعية (۳). جمهور
۲. ذهب الحنفية إلى أنه إذا قصد بلبسه الخاتم التجبر والاستعلاء كره، وإن لم يقصده لَمْ يكره، ومع ذَلِكَ فإن تركه لِمَنْ لا يحتاج إلى الختم أفضل، ولا كراهة عندهم في لبسه للزينة إذا خلا من محذور .. الأولى أن يَكُون الخاتم أقل من المثقال؛ لأنَّه أبعد عن السرف. وبـه قـال ابن الملك.
(٤)
٤. ذهب بعض الشافعية إلى تحريم لبس خاتم الفضة للرجل إذا زاد على المثقال. ه كراهة لبس خاتم الفضة لكل مكلف، ذي سلطان أو غيره، حكاه ابن عبد البر عن بعض أهل العلم من غير تعيين (۱).
٦. خص أهل الشام الكراهة بغير ذوي السلطان(٢).
يجوز اتخاذ خاتم الفضة للرجل، بَلْ يندب بشرط نية الاقتداء بالنبي ، ويحرم لبسه إذا أدى إلى العجب. وإليه ذهب المالكية (۳).
Chapter One: Disagreement in Chain of Transmission. Ibn Hajar went to a fourth interpretation: He (the Prophet) took a gold ring for adornment and people followed him in it, so it was prohibited and he threw it away, and said: 'I will never wear it.' So people threw away their rings following him. Then he needed a ring for sealing, so he took one of silver and engraved his noble name on it. People again followed him with those engraved rings, so he threw it away until people threw away those engraved rings so that the benefit of engraving his name would not be lost due to shared names. When all their rings were gone, he returned to his own specific ring and began wearing it. The Effect of Hadith on Juristic Disagreement: Ruling on Wearing Silver Rings for Men: The jurists differed on the ruling of wearing silver rings for men as follows: 1. The majority of scholars from earlier and later times permitted taking a silver ring, whether for a ruler or otherwise. The Shafi'is also said this. 2. The Hanafis said: If one intends by wearing the ring arrogance and self-exaltation, it is disliked (makruh); if he does not intend that, it is not disliked. Nevertheless, leaving it for those who do not need sealing is better. There is no dislike in wearing it for adornment if free from prohibition. It is preferable that the ring be less than a mithqal, as that is further from extravagance. Ibn al-Malik said this. 4. Some Shafi'is said wearing a silver ring for men is forbidden if it exceeds a mithqal. Dislike of wearing a silver ring for every legally responsible person, ruler or otherwise, was narrated by Ibn Abd al-Barr from some scholars without specification. 6. The people of Sham restricted the dislike to non-rulers. It is permissible to take a silver ring for a man, rather it is recommended with the intention of following the Prophet, and it is forbidden if it leads to vanity. The Malikis held this.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a dispute analysis of the ruling on men wearing silver rings, demonstrating how different interpretations of Prophetic actions lead to juristic disagreement (ikhtilaf). The Hanafi position is nuanced: wearing a silver ring is permissible in itself, but disliked (makruh) if the wearer intends to show off or dominate. If worn simply for adornment or for sealing letters (a practical need), there is no dislike. The Hanafis also recommend the ring to be less than one mithqal (approx. 4.25 grams) to avoid extravagance. This contrasts with the majority view (including Shafi'is) which permits it absolutely, and with some Shafi'is who forbid it if it exceeds a mithqal. The Malikis consider it recommended (mandub) if one intends to follow the Sunnah, but forbidden if it leads to vanity. This shows that a single hadith (the Prophet's wearing and then discarding a ring) can be interpreted in multiple ways, each leading to a different ruling.

Key Terms
خاتم الفضة (khatam al-fidda): A ring made of silver worn by men
المثقال (al-mithqal): A unit of weight approximately 4.25 grams

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Forbidden (haram)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Permissible (mubah) and better to leave if no need
D) Recommended (mandub)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school balances permissibility with intention and social context, demonstrating how usul principles allow for nuanced rulings.

Evaluating Evidence in Fiqh: The Case of Fasting in the Second Half of Sha'ban
Presents four madhhab positions with proof and critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a weak hadith cannot establish prohibition or dislike, and state that the default is permissibility until strong evidence arises.

Core Idea
A weak hadith cannot override the default of permissibility; the original ruling remains until strong evidence proves otherwise.

This passage presents a classic fiqh dispute where the strength of a hadith determines the ruling. Understanding how Hanafi usul evaluates conflicting evidence is essential for sound ijtihad. We will analyse the positions on fasting in the second half of Sha'ban, the proof cited, and the majority's critique using the usul principle of default permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the positions
Recognize that the conflict is about fasting in the second half of Sha'ban: some say haram or makruh, while the majority (Hanafi included) say permissible.
The text mentions Al-Ruyani (Shafi'i) for prohibition and the majority for dislike or permissibility.
2
Identify the evidence for the prohibitive positions
The first three schools (implied) rely on a hadith from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-'Ala'.
The hadith is not quoted but its chain is critiqued.
3
Analyse the majority's critique of the evidence
The majority declares the hadith weak and argues that it cannot justify prohibition or dislike. They also note that there is evidence supporting recommendation (istihbab).
The critique focuses on the sanad (chain of transmission).
4
Apply the usul principle of default permissibility
The majority invokes the usul maxim 'al-asl al-jawaz' (the basic principle is permissibility) until a strong proof of prohibition or dislike is established.
In Hanafi usul, for mu'amalat and acts not explicitly commanded, default is ibaha; for acts of worship, default is hazr, but this is debated. Here, the text asserts permissibility.
5
State the preponderant view and its basis
The majority view (permitting fasting) is preponderant (rajih) because the prohibition evidence is weak and the default is permissibility.
This reflects the Hanafi tarjih methodology: weigh proofs by authenticity and strength of dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شعبان (۱). الفصل الأول / الاختلاف في السند
ثالثاً: ذهب الروياني(٢) من الشافعية إلى تحريم صوم النصف الثاني من شعبان).
رابعاً: ذهب جمهور كراهة (٤).
العلماء إلى إباحة صوم النصف الثاني من شعبان من غير
واستدل أصحاب المذاهب الثلاثة الأول بحديث عَبْد الرَّحْمن بن العلاء، عَلَى
اختلاف في تحديد نوع الحكم.
وأجاب الجمهور بتضعيف حديثه، وعدم وجود ما يقتضي التحريم أو الكراهة،
بَلْ وجود ما يعضد القول بالاستحباب.
ومذهب الجمهور هُوَ الراجح في عدم الكراهة وجواز صيام النصف الثاني من شعبان لضعف حَدِيث العلاء وعدم صحته. والأصل الجواز حَتَّى يأتي دليل التحريم

Sha'ban (1). Chapter One / Difference in the Chain. Third: Al-Ruyani from the Shafi'is held that fasting in the second half of Sha'ban is prohibited. Fourth: The majority held dislike. The scholars permitted fasting in the second half of Sha'ban without (dislike). And the proponents of the first three schools argued with the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-‘Ala’, with a difference in specifying the type of ruling. The majority responded by declaring his hadith weak, and the absence of anything necessitating prohibition or dislike; rather, there is what supports the view of recommendation. The majority view is the preponderant one in negating dislike and permitting fasting in the second half of Sha'ban because of the weakness of the hadith of al-‘Ala’ and its lack of authenticity. The basic principle is permissibility until evidence of prohibition or dislike comes.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a fiqh dispute on fasting in the second half of Sha'ban. The author lists three schools (apparently the first two are missing from the OCR but implied) and then two additional positions: Al-Ruyani (Shafi'i) holds it is haram (prohibited), and the majority opinion holds it is makruh (disliked) or permissible. The evidence used by the first three schools is a hadith from ‘Abd al-Rahman ibn al-‘Ala’. The majority responds by declaring that hadith weak and argues that there is no proof for prohibition or dislike; rather, there is evidence supporting recommendation. The author concludes that the majority view is rajih (preponderant) because the hadith is weak and the original ruling is permissibility (ibaha) until evidence for prohibition arises. This follows the Hanafi usul principle that the default in acts of worship is prohibition unless there is clear evidence, but here the original ruling is permissibility due to lack of strong prohibition evidence.

Key Terms
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike (makruh) – a ruling that it is better to avoid but not sinful.
التحريم (al-tahrim): Prohibition (haram) – a binding prohibition.
الاستحباب (al-istihbab): Recommendation (mustahabb) – encouraged but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
A weak hadith cannot change the default permissibility; the majority view prevails because it adheres to the usul principle of relying on authentic evidence.

Hadith Dispute and Fiqh Divergence: Combining Prayers During Travel
Presents three juristic opinions on combining prayers during travel and their evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principal hadith used to support combining prayers during travel, evaluate its chain criticism according to Hanafi usul, and explain the resulting three juristic positions.

Core Idea
A single hadith chain can determine a widespread fiqh practice—but if it is mu'allal (flawed), the practice collapses.

The permissibility of combining prayers during travel is a contested issue. At the heart of the disagreement lies a hadith of Muadh ibn Jabal. This lesson examines how Hanafi usul scholars critique that hadith and how that critique leads to a distinct Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed hadith
Locate the hadith of Muadh ibn Jabal reported via Qutaybah from Layth. This hadith is the primary evidence for the majority's permissibility of combining prayers during travel.
2
Understand the Hanafi critique
Hanafi usul scholars, following hadith critics like Abu Dawud and Ibn Hajar, declare the hadith mu'allal (flawed) due to tafarrud—Qutaybah being the sole narrator from Layth. This weakens the hadith's authenticity.
A mu'allal hadith is not accepted for establishing a ruling that contradicts established practice (like praying each prayer on time).
3
See the impact on fiqh opinions
Because the hadith is weak, the Hanafi school does not accept general combining during travel. The first opinion (majority) relies on this hadith; the second (Hanafi) rejects it; the third (some Malikis and Hanbalis) accepts only combining in delay.
4
Relate to usul principles
The Hanafi position illustrates a key usul rule: a hadith with weak chain (zanni al-thubut) cannot override a clear default (asl) or a stronger evidence (like the Quranic command to pray at appointed times).
This is an example of tarjih: the Hanafis prefer the stronger evidence (the default practice) over the weak hadith.

الفصل الأول الاختلاف في السند
قَالَ أبو داود: «ليس في جمع التقديم حَدِيث قائم ).
وَقَالَ ابن حجر: «والمشهور في جمع التقديم ما أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وأحمد وابن حبان من طريق الليث، عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن أبي الطفيل، عن معاذ بن جبل. وَقَدْ أعله جَمَاعَة من أئمة الْحَدِيث بتفرد قتيبة عن الليث».
أثر الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء (الجمع بين الصلاتين)
اختلف الفقهاء في حكم الجمع بين الصلاتين بعذر السفر على أقوال هي: الأول: يجوز الجمع بَيْنَ الظهر والعصر في وقت أيهما شاء تقديماً أو تأخيراً، وكذا المغرب والعشاء، وَهُوَ قَوْل جمهور العلماء مِنْهُمْ: سعيد بن زيد، وسعد، وأسامة، ومعاذ بن جبل، وأبو موسى، وابن عَبَّاسٍ، وابن عمر. وبه قَالَ طاووس، ومجاهد، وعكرمة، والثوري، وإسحاق، وأبو ثور، وابن المنذر). وإليه ذهب مالك في المشهور عَنْهُ، والشافعية، وأحمد في أصح الروايتين، والهادوية من الزيدية.
الثاني: لا يجوز الجمع بَيْنَ فرضين في حال من الأحوال، إلا الظهر والعصر للحاج جمع تقديم بعرفة والمغرب والعشاء تأخيراً بمزدلفة، وهذا الجمع بسبب النسك لا بسبب السفر. وبه قال الحسن البصري، وابن سيرين، والنخعي، ومكحول، وإليه ذهب أبو حَنِيفَة وعامة أصحابه.
الثالث: يجوز الجمع بَيْنَ الظهر والعصر، أو بَيْنَ المغرب والعشاء جمع تأخير لا تقديم. وَهُوَ قَوْل الأوزاعي في إحدى الروايتين عَنْهُ. وإليه ذهب الإمام أحمد في رِوَايَة، ومالك في رِوَايَة ابن القاسم واختياره، وَهُوَ ظاهر مذهب ابن حزم.
واستدل أصحاب المذهب الأول بحديث معاذ من رواية قتيبة، وَقَدْ تبين عدم صحته.
First Chapter: Disagreement in the Chain. Abu Dawud said: 'There is no established hadith regarding combining in advance.' Ibn Hajar said: 'The well-known hadith regarding combining in advance is the one reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ahmad, and Ibn Hibban from the chain of Layth, from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Abu al-Tufayl, from Muadh ibn Jabal. A group of hadith scholars have declared it flawed due to Qutaybah being the sole narrator from Layth.' The effect of hadith on disagreement of jurists (combining the two prayers). The jurists disagreed on the ruling of combining the two prayers due to the excuse of travel into the following opinions: First: It is permissible to combine Zuhr and Asr at any time they wish, either advancing or delaying, and likewise Maghrib and Isha. This is the opinion of the majority of scholars, among them: Sa'id ibn Zayd, Sa'd, Usamah, Muadh ibn Jabal, Abu Musa, Ibn Abbas, and Ibn Umar. And it is the opinion of Tawus, Mujahid, Ikrimah, al-Thawri, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, and Ibn al-Mundhir. And it is the opinion of Malik in the well-known narration from him, and of the Shafi'is, and Ahmad in the more correct of the two narrations, and the Hadawiyyah of the Zaydiyyah. Second: It is not permissible to combine two obligatory prayers under any circumstances, except Zuhr and Asr for the pilgrim as combining in advance at Arafa, and Maghrib and Isha as combining in delay at Muzdalifah. This combining is due to the ritual not due to travel. This is the opinion of al-Hasan al-Basri, Ibn Sirin, al-Nakha'i, Makhul, and it is the opinion of Abu Hanifah and his companions in general. Third: It is permissible to combine Zuhr and Asr, or Maghrib and Isha, as combining in delay, not advance. This is the opinion of al-Awza'i in one of the two narrations from him. And it is the opinion of Imam Ahmad in one narration, and Malik in the narration of Ibn al-Qasim and his choice, and it is the apparent school of Ibn Hazm. The proponents of the first opinion adduced the hadith of Muadh from the narration of Qutaybah, and its lack of authenticity has become clear.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a usul dispute: how the authenticity of a hadith chain leads to differing fiqh rulings. The author begins by citing two hadith critics: Abu Dawud states there is no authentic hadith for combining prayers in advance (taqdim), while Ibn Hajar notes that the famous hadith of Muadh is declared flawed (mu'allal) because Qutaybah was the sole narrator from Layth. This sets the stage for the ikhtilaf. The first opinion (majority, including Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad in one narration) permits combining both in advance and delay for any travel. The second opinion (Abu Hanifah and his companions) restricts combining to only the Hajj context: at Arafa (taqdim) and Muzdalifa (ta'khir). The third opinion (al-Awza'i, Ahmad in another narration, Malik via Ibn al-Qasim, and Ibn Hazm) permits only combining in delay, not advance. The Hanafi position is that general travel does not justify altering prayer times beyond what is prescribed, and the hadith of Muadh is not reliable. Thus, the usul principle at play is that a hadith whose chain is weak (due to tafarrud) cannot override established practices (like praying each prayer in its own time). The Hanafis follow the stronger evidence, which is the default that prayers must be observed at their appointed times unless a clear, authentic exception exists.

Key Terms
جمع التقديم (jamʿ al-taqdīm): Combining two prayers by performing the later one at the time of the earlier one.
جمع التأخير (jamʿ al-taʾkhīr): Combining two prayers by performing the earlier one at the time of the later one.
الحديث المعلل (al-ḥadīth al-muʿallal): A hadith with a hidden flaw that undermines its authenticity, here due to Tafarrud (sole narration) of Qutaybah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is combining prayers (jama') between Zuhr and Asr permissible?
A) During any travel
B) Only at Arafa during Hajj, as combining in advance
C) Only at Muzdalifa, as combining in delay
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a hadith declared mu'allal due to tafarrud cannot establish a ruling that contradicts the default, thus combining prayers during general travel is not permitted.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Three Opinions on Combining Prayers: How Hadith Authenticity Shapes Juristic Disagreement
Parsing three juristic views on combining prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three major positions on combining prayers and evaluate how each school's methodology regarding hadith authenticity leads to its ruling.

Core Idea
Combining prayers is permitted only in Hajj according to Hanafis, reflecting their stricter criteria for hadith authenticity.

This passage presents three distinct juristic positions on combining two obligatory prayers (jam' bayn al-salatayn). It illustrates a core principle of Usul al-Fiqh: differences in evaluating the authenticity and interpretation of hadith lead to substantial legal disagreement. Understanding this helps us appreciate the methodological rigor behind each school's ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the passage and list the three opinions: (1) combining is generally permissible, (2) combining is only allowed for the pilgrim at Arafah and Muzdalifah, (3) combining is allowed as a delay only (jam' ta'khir) for Zuhr/Asr and Maghrib/Isha.
The first opinion is attributed to Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad; the second to Abu Hanifah and early scholars; the third to al-Awza'i and others.
2
Note the proponents and their evidence
Identify which scholars support each view. For the first view, the author cites a hadith of Mu'adh. For the Hanafi view, no specific hadith is mentioned here, but it is based on the practice of the Prophet during Hajj and the general rule of praying each prayer on time.
The passage explicitly states that the hadith used by the first group has been proven unsound.
3
Understand the Hanafi position's usul basis
The Hanafi ruling restricts combining to the Hajj rituals because the only authentic evidence for combining is the Prophet's action at Arafah and Muzdalifah. Other reported hadiths (like that of Mu'adh) are considered weak in the Hanafi methodology, so they do not override the general obligation to pray each prayer in its prescribed time.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that acts of worship are tawqeefi (only as established by sound evidence).
4
Analyze the usul principle: hadith evaluation causes ikhtilaf
The disagreement arises because schools differ in their criteria for accepting hadith. The first school (Maliki, Shafi'i, etc.) accepted the hadith of Mu'adh, while the Hanafis deemed it unsound. This shows how a difference in evaluating the chain of transmission (sanad) leads to divergent rulings.
This is a classic example of how usul al-fiqh principles—specifically, rules for hadith authentication—directly impact fiqh rulings.

(المنذر) وإليه ذهب مالك في المشهور عنه، والشافعية، وأحمد في أصح الروايتين، والهادوية من الزيدية. الثاني: لا يجوز الجمع بين فرضين في حال من الأحوال، إلا الظهر والعصر للحاج جمع تقديم بعرفة والمغرب والعشاء تأخيراً بمزدلفة، وهذا الجمع بسبب النسك لا بسبب السفر. وبه قال الحسن البصري، وابن سيرين، والنخعي، ومكحول، وإليه ذهب أبو حنيفة وعامة أصحابه. الثالث: يجوز الجمع بين الظهر والعصر، أو بين المغرب والعشاء جمع تأخير لا تقديم. وهو قول الأوزاعي في إحدى الروايتين عنه. وإليه ذهب الإمام أحمد في رواية، ومالك في رواية ابن القاسم واختياره، وهو ظاهر مذهب ابن حزم. واستدل أصحاب المذهب الأول بحديث معاذ من رواية قتيبة، وقد تبين عدم صحته.
Chapter One: Disagreement in the Chain. (Al-Mundhir) and to him went Malik in the famous view, and al-Shafi'iyyah, and Ahmad in the most correct of the two narrations, and al-Hadawiyyah from the Zaydiyyah. Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct positions on the ruling of combining two obligatory prayers (jam' bayn al-salatayn). The first position, attributed to Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad in one view, and others, permits combining in all cases. The second position, held by Abu Hanifah and a group of early scholars, restricts combining to the specific rituals of Hajj only, not for travel generally. The third position, held by al-Awza'i, Ahmad in another narration, and Malik in a different transmission, allows only delay combine (jam' ta'khir) but not advance combine. The author notes that the first group used the hadith of Mu'adh but it has been proven unsound. This illustrates how differences in the authenticity and interpretation of hadith lead to juristic disagreement.

Key Terms
جمع (jam'): Combining two obligatory prayers into one time slot.
فرض (fard): An obligatory act of worship established by definitive evidence.
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting a hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They considered travel to be a form of hardship that still does not permit combining.
B) They found the hadith of Mu'adh to be inauthentic and thus did not accept general combination.
C) They followed the opinion of Ibn Sirin and al-Hasan al-Basri blindly.
D) They used qiyas to compare travel to Hajj and concluded it is not analogous.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits combining prayers only during Hajj rituals, reflecting its stringent criteria for the authenticity of hadith evidence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
