8 min
The Kaffarah Dispute: Sequential vs Optional – How Hadith Wording Drives Ikhtilaf
Presents the ikhtilāf among madhāhib regarding the kaffārah for breaking fast in Ramaḍān

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the difference in the conjunctive word (thumma vs. aw) in the ḥadīth of kaffārah leads to two opposing rulings on the sequence of expiation, and evaluate the Hanafī method of preferring the sequential order.

Core Idea
The same ḥadīth transmitted with 'then' versus 'or' produces divergent rulings on whether kaffārah is sequential or optional.

This passage presents a classic example of ikhtilāf rooted in textual variation. The kaffārah for intentionally breaking the Ramaḍān fast through intercourse is reported in different versions of the same ḥadīth: one with 'thumma' (then) implying sequence, another with 'aw' (or) implying choice. By working through this dispute, you will see how the Hanafī school relies on the principle that imperative sequential commands indicate order (tartīb), while the Mālikī school gives the word 'aw' its literal force of choice (takhyīr). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze ḥadīth-based rulings through  uṣūl al-fiqh lenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Extract from the passage the Hanafī (and majority) position that the kaffārah must be sequential: free a slave, then fast two months, then feed sixty poor. Also extract the Mālikī position that allows any of the three as a choice.
2
Locate the key textual difference
The Hanafīs rely on a ḥadīth version using 'thumma' (then) to indicate order. The Mālikīs rely on a version using 'aw' (or). Note that the passage quotes the Mālikī version: 'أن يكفر بعتق رقبة أو صيام شهرين متتابعين أو إطعام ستين مسكيناً'.
The Hanafī version is not quoted in this passage but is well-known in other sources.
3
Analyse the linguistic implications in uṣūl al-fiqh
In Hanafī uṣūl, 'thumma' indicates tartīb (order) and 'aw' indicates takhyīr (choice). However, Hanafīs also hold that 'aw' can be used for listing options in a preferred order (tartīb al-dhikr) unless there is a qarīna (context) indicating otherwise. The Mālikīs take 'aw' at face value for equal choice.
This step connects the linguistic analysis to uṣūl principles.
4
Evaluate the Hanafī tarjīḥ mechanism
The Hanafī school prefers the narration with 'thumma' because it is more established (ṣaḥīḥ) and because the imperative command (amr) in the ḥadīth is understood to imply wujūb and order. The version with 'aw' is considered a riwāyah bi al-maʿnā (transmission by meaning) and is less precise.
The passage does not provide the full Hanafī response, but this is the typical argument.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilāf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafī fiqh, the expiator must follow the sequence; if he skips to fasting without attempting to free a slave, his kaffārah is not valid. Under Mālikī, any of the three is valid immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
يتفرع على هذا أيضاً اختلاف الفقهاء في الكفارة هل هي على الترتيب أم على التخيير؟ اختلف الفقهاء في تحديد الكفارة على من أفطر عامداً في رمضان هل هي مقيدة بالترتيب أم أنها على التخيير؟ فذهب أبو حنيفة، والأوزاعي، والثوري، والشافعي، وأحمد في أصح الروايتين عنه، إلى أنّ الكفارة مقيدة على الترتيب الوارد في الحديث، فهي عتق رقبة، فإن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين، فإن لم يستطع، فإطعام ستين مسكيناً، وهو مذهب الظاهرية، والزيدية. في حين ذهب مالك وأصحابه، وأحمد في رواية عنه إلى أنّ الكفارة على التخيير، أي: أنه مخير بين العتق أو الصيام أو الإطعام بأيّها كفر فقد أوفى، واستدلوا برواية مالك وابن جريج ومن تابعهم لحديث أبي هريرة (أمره رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يكفر بعتق رقبة أو صيام شهرين متتابعين أو إطعام ستين مسكيناً)، و(أو) هنا تقتضي التخيير، واختار مالك الإطعام؛ لأنه يشبه البدل من الصيام، فقال مالك: «الإطعام أحب إلي في العتق وغيره»، وعنه في رواية أخرى - جواباً لسائله : «الطعام، لا نعرف غير الطعام لا يأخذ مالك بالعتق ولا بالصيام». وذهب الحسن البصري إلى التخيير بين العتق ونحر بدنة، واستدل بحديث أرسله هو أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال في الذي وطئ امرأته في رمضان: رقبة ثم بدنة». وروي عن الشعبي، والزهري أن من أفطر في رمضان عامداً فإن عليه عتق رقبة، أو إطعام ستين مسكيناً، أو صيام شهرين متتابعين مع قضاء اليوم. قال ابن عبد البر: وفي قول الشعبي والزهري ما يقضي لرواية مالك بالتخيير في الحديث.
Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text. This also branches into the disagreement of the jurists regarding the expiation: is it sequential or optional? The jurists differed concerning determining the expiation for whoever intentionally broke the fast in Ramadan: is it restricted to a sequence or is it optional? Abu Hanifa, al-Awza'i, al-Thawri, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad in the most correct of the two narrations from him held that the expiation is restricted to the sequence mentioned in the hadith: it is freeing a slave; if he does not find, then fasting two consecutive months; if he is unable, then feeding sixty poor persons. This is also the madhhab of the Zahiris and Zaydis. Meanwhile, Malik and his companions, and Ahmad in one narration from him, held that the expiation is optional, i.e., he may choose between freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding, and whichever he uses to expiate, he has fulfilled. They adduced the narration of Malik and Ibn Jurayj and those who followed them of the hadith of Abu Hurayra: 'he commanded him to expiate by freeing a slave OR fasting two consecutive months OR feeding sixty poor persons' – and 'or' here indicates choice. Malik chose feeding because it resembles a substitute for fasting, so Malik said: 'Feeding is more beloved to me than freeing etc.' And from him in another narration, answering a questioner: 'Feeding, we know nothing other than feeding. Malik does not adopt freeing or fasting.' Al-Hasan al-Basri went to choice between freeing a slave and sacrificing a camel, adducing a hadith he transmitted as mursal that the Prophet said to the one who had intercourse with his wife in Ramadan: 'A slave then a camel.' And it is narrated from al-Sha'bi and al-Zuhri that whoever intentionally broke the fast in Ramadan must free a slave, or feed sixty poor persons, or fast two consecutive months along with making up the day. Ibn Abd al-Barr said: In the statement of al-Sha'bi and al-Zuhri there is support for Malik's narration of choice in the hadith.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilāf) among the major madhāhib regarding the kaffārah for one who intentionally breaks the fast in Ramaḍān without a valid excuse, specifically through sexual intercourse. The author presents two main positions. The first position, held by the majority including Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis (in the stronger narration), is that the kaffārah is sequential: first free a slave; if unable, fast two consecutive months; if unable, feed sixty poor persons. This is based on the well-known hadith of the Bedouin who had intercourse, where the Prophet ordered in that order. The second position, held by Mālik and some Hanbalīs, is that the three options are entirely optional; one may choose any of them. They rely on a version of the hadith that uses the word 'or' (aw), which linguistically indicates choice. The author also notes that Mālik himself preferred feeding. Other opinions include those of al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī and the tābiʿūn al-Shaʿbī and al-Zuhrī, which lean toward choice. The passage illustrates how differences in the wording of a ḥadīth (with sequential 'then' vs. 'or') lead to different fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation required for violating a fast of Ramaḍān intentionally through intercourse.
الترتيب (al-tartīb): The sequential order of the three expiatory acts: free a slave, then fast, then feed.
التخيير (al-takhyīr): Free choice among the three expiatory acts without sequence.
أو (aw): Conjunction meaning 'or'; in uṣūl, it generally indicates choice (takhyīr) but can be overridden by context.
ثم (thumma): Conjunction meaning 'then'; indicates sequential order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī madhhab, what is the order of expiation for intentionally breaking the fast in Ramaḍān through intercourse?
A) Free a slave, then fast two months, then feed sixty poor persons.
B) Either free a slave or fast or feed, at one's choice.
C) Feed sixty poor persons only.
D) Fast two months, then free a slave, then feed.

Key Takeaway
Variation in a single ḥadīth wording (thumma vs. aw) leads to a legitimate ikhtilāf on whether kaffārah is sequential or optional, demonstrating the critical role of ḥadīth criticism and uṣūl al-fiqh in deriving rulings.

Maintenance for the Irrevocably Divorced Woman: Textual Interpretation in Usul al-Fiqh
Presents three opinions on maintenance and housing for a non-pregnant irrevocably divorced woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul principle underlying each school's position and evaluate the Hanafi response to the opposing dalil in the dispute on maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman.

Core Idea
The dispute between a general Quranic command and a specific hadith is resolved by different usul principles: the Hanafis prioritize the Quran over a contradictory hadith.

This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: whether a general Quranic command (amr) can be overridden by a specific hadith. The case of maintenance for an irrevocably divorced woman illustrates how different schools apply the principles of takhsīs (specification) and tarjīḥ (preference). By studying this dispute, you will understand how usul principles directly shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three opinions on maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (suknā) for an irrevocably divorced non-pregnant woman: 1) Both maintenance and housing (Hanafi and others). 2) Neither (Ahmad, Zahiris, etc.). 3) Housing only (seven jurists, al-Awza'i, etc.).
Note which opinion is the Hanafi position.
2
Extract the Hanafi dalil and the opposing dalil
The Hanafis rely on the general command in Surah al-Talaq (65:1,6): 'House them from where you live.' The opposing school cites the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays that the Prophet said the irrevocably divorced woman has no maintenance or housing.
Identify the specific texts: Quran versus hadith.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject the hadith because it contradicts the explicit Quranic text. They quote Umar's statement: 'We do not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the saying of a woman.'
This is a tarjīḥ (preference) of Quran over a contradictory hadith.
4
Contrast the opposing view's usul
The opposing school considers the hadith as a takhsīs (specification) of the general Quranic command. They argue that authentic specific hadith can specify general Quranic verses, a common usul principle.
The difference is whether the hadith truly contradicts or specifies.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and Hanafi tarjih
The practical difference: Hanafis require maintenance and housing; others do not. The Hanafi tarjih is based on the principle that a clear Quranic text (nass) cannot be overridden by a hadith that contradicts it, unless there is a valid resolution (e.g., naskh or jam').
This illustrates the hierarchy of sources in Hanafi usul.

And the jurists differed regarding the woman divorced thrice (the non-revocably divorced woman) when she is not pregnant: is maintenance and housing obligatory for her or not? Upon three opinions: The first opinion: that the irrevocably divorced woman who is not pregnant, maintenance and housing are obligatory upon the divorcing husband. That is reported from Umar, Ibn Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Aisha, al-Nakha'i, Ibn Shubruma, al-Thawri, al-Hasan ibn Salih, Uthman al-Batti, Ubayd Allah ibn al-Hasan al-Anbari. And it is a narration from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib. And the Hanafis said it. And they used as evidence that Allah opened Surah al-Talaq with His saying: 'O Prophet, when you divorce women, divorce them for their waiting period,' for the address includes the revocably divorced and the irrevocably divorced. Then when He said after that in verses: 'House them from where you live out of your means,' it was a command including all, so it included the irrevocably divorced and the revocably divorced and they are equal in the ruling: regarding the obligation of housing. And they responded to the hadith of Fatimah that it contradicts the explicit text of the Quran, and based on this, Umar ibn al-Khattab rejected her hadith, as al-Tahawi narrated from him that he said: 'We do not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the saying of a woman, we do not know whether she spoke truth or lied.' The second opinion: There is no maintenance at all nor housing for the irrevocably divorced non-pregnant woman. That is reported from Ali, Ibn Abbas, Jabir, Tawus, Amr ibn Maymun, al-Zuhri, Ikrimah, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, and Dawud. And it is a narration from al-Hasan al-Basri, Ata, and al-Sha'bi, and Ahmad went to it in the famous view of his school, and the Zahiris and the Imamiyyah said it. And they used as evidence the hadith of Fatimah, and they said there is no contradiction between it and the texts of the Book, and it is a sound hadith, explicit in its indication, and it is considered a specification for the general verses of spending and housing for waiting women, and it is not strange that the Prophetic Sunnah specifies the general of the Quran or restricts its absolute, as is known in usul al-fiqh. The third opinion: She has housing without maintenance. This is reported from the seven jurists, al-Awza'i, and Ibn Abi Layla, and it is a narration from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, al-Hasan, Ata, and al-Sha'bi.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known fiqh dispute regarding the entitlements of a woman who has been given an irrevocable third divorce (bā'in baynūna kubrā) and is not pregnant. The Hanafis hold the first opinion, based on the general command in Surah al-Talaq (65:1,6) which orders housing for all divorced women during their iddah. They argue that the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, which states that the irrevocably divorced woman has no right to maintenance or housing, contradicts the explicit Quranic text and therefore cannot override it. They substantiate this by the action of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who rejected the hadith on the grounds that it conflicted with the Quran and Sunnah. The second opinion, held by Ahmad ibn Hanbal (in the famous view), the Zahiris, and others, relies on the hadith of Fatimah, considering it a specification (takhsīs) of the general Quranic verses, which is a valid principle in usul al-fiqh. The third opinion, attributed to the seven jurists of Medina and others, compromises by granting housing but not maintenance. The author shows how differences in textual interpretation, specifically the handling of a general Quranic command versus a specific hadith, lead to varying rulings.

Key Terms
المطلقة البائن بينونة كبرى (al-muṭallaqah al-bāʾin baynūnah kubrā): A woman divorced by three pronouncements, making her irrevocably separated from her husband; she cannot remarry him without an intervening marriage (tahlīl).
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and other basic needs, provided by the husband during the iddah period.
السكنى (al-suknā): Housing or lodging provided by the husband for the divorced woman during her iddah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that a general Quranic command cannot be overridden by a contradictory hadith, even if sahih, unless the contradiction can be resolved by recognized usul methods.

Dispute on Maintenance for a Divorced Woman: Takhsis of Quran by Sunnah
Presents responses and two minority opinions on maintenance for a divorced woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on maintenance and housing for a mubtatta woman and apply the Hanafi usul principle that an ahad hadith cannot specify a general Quranic text if it contradicts an explicit Quranic ruling.

Core Idea
A hadith that contradicts clear Quran cannot specify it – the Quran is the primary authority.

This passage illustrates a classic usul dispute: how to reconcile the hadith of Fatima bint Qays with the general Quranic verses on maintenance during 'iddah. The Hanafi school argues that the hadith, being a single narration (ahad), cannot override the explicit Quranic command. Other schools treat the hadith as a specificator (mukhasis) of the general verses, leading to different rulings. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the underlying usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions on maintenance/housing
The passage presents two opinions clearly; the first (Hanafi) is missing from this excerpt. List them: 1) Full maintenance and housing (not in this passage), 2) No maintenance or housing, 3) Housing only.
The first opinion is the Hanafi position: maintenance and housing during 'iddah.
2
Isolate the proof for the second opinion
The second opinion relies on the hadith of Fatima bint Qays. Examine how they use it as a mukhasis for the general Quranic verses on maintenance and housing.
The hadith is considered sahih and explicit by its proponents.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject the hadith because it contradicts the explicit Quran. Note Umar's statement: 'We do not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the statement of a woman.'
The rejection is not based on weakness but on contradiction with a stronger source.
4
Understand the usul principle at play
The principle of takhsis (specification) of 'amm by ahad hadith. Hanafis accept it in principle but require that the hadith not contradict a clear nass. Here the Quranic verse (65:6) is clear and general, so the hadith is insufficient to specify.
This is a key difference in usul between Hanafis and others.
5
Evaluate the third opinion's basis
The third opinion (housing but no maintenance) tries to reconcile: housing is confirmed by other evidence, but maintenance is denied based on the hadith. This shows a middle ground.
This opinion is adopted by many schools including Malikis and Shafi'is.

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute about the legal ruling on maintenance and housing for a divorced woman whose marriage has been consummated and is not pregnant (mubtatta). The response argues that the hadith contradicts the clear Quranic verses on maintenance during 'iddah. The first opinion (full maintenance and housing) is missing from this excerpt, likely presented earlier. This illustrates how textual disagreement (ikhtilaf fi al-matn) leads to juristic differences.

Key Terms

مخصص (mukhaśśiś): A specificator that restricts the general meaning of a text.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): The act of specifying a general text (amm) by a specificator (mukhasis).

Judgment With Oath and One Witness: A Usul Dispute
Introduces dispute on plaintiff's oath substituting for one witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first of four opinions on judgment by one witness and an oath, explain the consensus on hudud, and analyse the hadith that grounds the default rule.

Core Idea
The hadith places the oath on the defendant; the dispute is whether the plaintiff's oath can substitute for a missing witness.

In this lesson, we examine a classical dispute rooted in the interpretation of the hadith 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر.' While all jurists agree on the default rule and on the exclusion of hudud, a key question arises: if the plaintiff cannot produce two witnesses, can his own oath compensate for one missing witness? The passage presents the first of four opinions, setting the stage for a deeper usul analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule from the hadith
From the hadith 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر,' derive the default evidentiary roles: plaintiff brings proof (two witnesses), defendant takes an oath to deny.
This is agreed upon by all schools.
2
Understand the area of consensus
All jurists agree that when the plaintiff brings full witnesses, judgment is given. They also agree that in hudud (fixed punishments), judgment cannot be based on one witness and an oath.
Hudud require rigorous proof; oath is insufficient.
3
Recognize the contested issue
When witness count is incomplete, the defendant's oath defaults. The dispute is whether the plaintiff's own oath can compensate for the missing witness, making the claim valid.
This is the point of ikhtilaf.
4
Map the first opinion
The first opinion (attributed to Ibn Hazm) holds that one witness plus the plaintiff's oath suffices in all cases outside hudud, including retaliation (qisas). This is a broad application.
The text presents only this opinion; three others are not detailed.
5
Apply usul analysis to the hadith
Use the dalil_block to see how the hadith is interpreted: the word 'on the denier' is seen as restrictive. The first opinion may view the plaintiff's oath as additional evidence allowed by other texts or by analogy.
This is the beginning of deeper usul reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الثاني: حكم القضاء باليمين مع الشاهد
إذا أقام المدعي نصاب الشهادة كاملاً، وقبل الْقَاضِي مِنْهُمْ شهاداتهم، حكم بِمَا ادعاه المدعي بلا خلاف بَيْنَ الْعُلَمَاء). وإذا لم يكتمل النصاب وطلب المدَّعِي يمين المدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ، فحلف المدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ دعوى المدعي؛ لأن اليمين للمدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ بقوله عَلَيْهِ الصَّلاة والسلام: «البَيِّنَة عَلَى المُدَّعِي واليمين عَلَى مَن أنكر». فإن حلف المدَّعِي فهل تقوم يمينه مقام النقص الحاصل في نصاب الشهادة؟ اتَّفق الفقهاء عَلَى أَنَّهُ لا يقضى باليمين والحالة هَذِهِ في الحدود، واختلفوا فِيْمَا سوى ذَلِكَ عَلَى أربعة مذاهب: الأول: يقضى بالشاهد مَعَ اليمين فيما سوى الحدود من غَيْر فرق بَيْنَ القصاص وسائر الحقوق، وبه قال ابن حزم).
The second model: The ruling on judgment with an oath and a witness. If the plaintiff presents a complete number of witnesses and the judge accepts their testimonies, he judges according to the plaintiff's claim without disagreement among scholars. If the number is incomplete and the plaintiff requests the defendant's oath, then the defendant swears (and the plaintiff's claim is dismissed) because the oath is for the defendant according to the saying of the Prophet (PBUH): 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' Then if the plaintiff swears, does his oath compensate for the deficiency in the number of witnesses? The jurists agree that judgment is not given with an oath in this situation in hudud, and they differ on matters other than hudud, having four schools: The first: judgment is given with one witness and an oath in all matters other than hudud, without distinction between retaliation and other rights, and Ibn Hazm adopted this.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage introduces a second example of textual disagreement leading to juristic differences: the issue of judging by one witness plus an oath. The author sets the context: if a plaintiff brings full witnesses, there is consensus that the judge rules for him. If the witness count is not complete, the defendant can take an oath to dismiss the claim. The question is whether the plaintiff's own oath can supplement the missing witness. There is agreement that in hudud (fixed punishments), an oath is insufficient. Beyond hudud, there are four opinions. The text here only presents the first opinion: one witness plus an oath suffices in all cases except hudud, even in retaliation, as held by Ibn Hazm. The other three opinions are not given in this excerpt. This illustrates how different schools interpret the hadith on burden of proof and oath, leading to differences in the rules of evidence.

Key Terms
اليمين مع الشاهد (al-yamin ma'a al-shahid): A legal procedure where a plaintiff's oath compensates for one missing witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which category of cases do all jurists agree that judgment cannot be given with an oath and one witness?
A) Hudud
B) Qisas
C) Financial transactions
D) Marriage contracts

Key Takeaway
The hadith establishes the default rule of proof, but the question of the plaintiff's oath creates a legitimate ikhtilaf; usul analysis clarifies the reasoning behind each position.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
How Different Readings of a Hadith Generate Disagreement: The Case of Judgment by Oath with One Witness
Presents the issue and three opinions on judgment by oath with one witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three positions on judgment by oath with one witness and explain how each position relates to the hadith 'al-bayyinah ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin ala man ankara' and the consensus on hudud.

Core Idea
A single hadith can yield multiple rulings when scholars differ on its scope and whether companion practice can override its apparent meaning.

This passage introduces a classic case of ikhtilaf: whether a plaintiff's oath can substitute for a missing witness. Although all scholars agree on the general rule that the oath is for the defendant, and that it does not apply in hudud, they split beyond that. By examining the three opinions presented, you will see how the same textual source can be interpreted differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed foundations
What do all scholars agree on? That with full witnesses judgment is made; that the oath is for the defendant; that oath with witness does not apply in hudud.
These points are not contested.
2
Examine the hadith's apparent meaning
The hadith 'al-bayyinah ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin ala man ankara' places the oath on the defendant. Why would some scholars allow the plaintiff to take an oath to supplement a missing witness? They must rely on other evidence or principles.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it can be overridden by stronger evidence.
3
Map the three positions and their scope
List each: (a) allowed in all non-hudud; (b) allowed except retaliation; (c) allowed only in financial matters.
These positions vary in scope from broad to narrow.
4
Identify the underlying methodological difference
The strict literalist (Ibn Hazm) may allow it based on other proofs; the Zaydis exclude retaliation because it resembles blood; the majority (Companions) restrict to financial matters, likely based on practice and istihsan.
The passage does not give full proofs for each, but the pattern suggests different usul approaches.
5
Connect to the larger usul principle
This shows how variants in matn understanding (or the use of other evidence) produce different rulings, even when the same hadith is cited.
The lesson demonstrates the impact of differing usul methodologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في الْمَتْن
النموذج الثاني:
حكم القضاء باليمين مع الشاهد
إذا أقام المدعي نصاب الشهادة كاملاً، وقبل الْقَاضِي منهم شهاداتهم، حكم بما ادعاه المدعي بلا خلاف بين العلماء. وإذا لم يكتمل النصاب وطلب المدَّعِي يمين المدَّعَى عليه، فحلف المدَّعَى عليه دعوى المدعي؛ لأن اليمين للمدَّعَى عليه بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر». فإن حلف المدَّعي فهل تقوم يمينه مقام النقص الحاصل في نصاب الشهادة؟ اتفق الفقهاء على أنه لا يقضى باليمين والحالة هذه في الحدود، واختلفوا فيما سوى ذلك على أربعة مذاهب:
الأول: يقضى بالشاهد مع اليمين فيما سوى الحدود من غير فرق بين القصاص وسائر الحقوق، وبه قال ابن حزم.
الثاني: يقضى به فيما سوى الحدود والقصاص، وهو قول الهادوية.
الثالث: يقضى به في الأموال فقط، روي عن الخلفاء الأربعة، وأُبي بن كعب، وابن عمر، وسعيد بن المسيب، والقاسم بن محمد، وأبي بكر بن عبد الرحمن، وخارجة بن زيد، وعبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عتبة، وسليمان بن يسار، والحسن، وشريح، وإياس بن معاوية، وعلي بن الحسين، ومحمد...
Chapter Two: Difference in the Text
The Second Model:
Ruling of Judgment by Oath with a Witness
If the plaintiff presents the complete number of witnesses, and the judge accepts their testimony, he judges in favor of what the plaintiff claimed, without disagreement among scholars. And if the number is incomplete and the plaintiff asks for the oath of the defendant, then the defendant swears (and thus rejects) the plaintiff's claim, because the oath is for the defendant according to his statement, peace be upon him: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' If the plaintiff swears, does his oath substitute for the deficiency in the number of witnesses? The jurists agreed that judgment is not made with the oath in this case for hudud, but they differed regarding other than that into four schools:
First: Judgment is made with one witness and an oath in all cases other than hudud, without distinction between retaliation and other rights, and this is the view of Ibn Hazm.
Second: Judgment is made with that in all cases other than hudud and retaliation, and this is the view of the Hadiyya.
Third: Judgment is made with it only in financial matters, transmitted from the four caliphs, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Ibn Umar, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, al-Qasim ibn Muhammad, Abu Bakr ibn Abd al-Rahman, Kharijah ibn Zayd, Ubaydullah ibn Abdullah ibn Utbah, Sulayman ibn Yasar, al-Hasan, Shurayh, Iyas ibn Mu'awiyah, Ali ibn al-Husayn, and Muhammad...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents the issue of judgment by oath with one witness, a classical point of disagreement among jurists due to different interpretations of hadith evidence. The author first establishes the general rule: if a plaintiff provides the full number of witnesses (two witnesses for most claims), the judge rules accordingly. If the number is incomplete, the defendant may take an oath to deny the claim, based on the well-known hadith. The question is whether the plaintiff's own oath can fill the gap. Jurists agree that in hudud cases (fixed punishments like theft or fornication) the oath does not substitute because of the principle of avoiding punishment through doubt. Beyond hudud, they differ into four positions. The first position, held by Ibn Hazm (Zahiri), allows it in all non-hudud cases including retaliation (qisas). The second position, of the Hadiyya (Zaydi school), excludes retaliation cases. The third position, attributed to many Companions and early jurists, restricts it to financial matters. The fourth opinion (not fully given here but typically the Hanafi view) also restricts it to financial matters, with additional conditions. The author uses these differences to illustrate how variant understandings of hadith texts (matn) lead to divergent rulings.

Key Terms
القضاء باليمين مع الشاهد (al-qaḍāʾ bi al-yamīn maʿa al-shāhid): Judgment made by accepting a single witness and an oath from the plaintiff as sufficient proof
نصاب الشهادة (niṣāb al-shahāda): The required number of witnesses to prove a claim, typically two men or one man and two women
الحدود (al-ḥudūd): Fixed punishments prescribed by divine law for specific crimes, e.g., theft, fornication

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third opinion mentioned in the passage, in which type of cases is judgment by oath with one witness accepted?
A) All cases except hudud
B) All cases except hudud and retaliation
C) Only financial cases
D) Only cases involving retaliation

Key Takeaway
Differences in understanding a single hadith, combined with the weight given to companion practice, generate valid ikhtilaf on whether a plaintiff's oath can supplement a missing witness.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Dispute on Oath with One Witness (al-Yamin ma'a al-Shahid)
Listing four juristic opinions on al-yamin ma'a al-shahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four opinions on al-yamin ma'a al-shahid as presented in this passage and distinguish the Hanafi position from the majority position.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school rejects the oath with one witness absolutely; the majority accepts it in financial matters, citing the Prophet's practice.

This passage from a usul al-fiqh text presents a clear example of how disagreement in the interpretation of textual evidence (ikhtilaf) leads to different fiqh positions. The issue is whether a plaintiff's oath combined with the testimony of a single witness can be sufficient for a judicial ruling. The passage lists four distinct opinions and attributes them to various scholars, schools, and generations. This lesson will guide you in parsing the opinions and understanding the key point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four opinions
Read the passage carefully and list the four opinions mentioned. The first opinion is not given in this excerpt; start with the second, third, and fourth.
The passage begins with 'the second' opinion, meaning the first was discussed earlier.
2
Attribute each opinion to schools or scholars
Note which scholars or madhahib are associated with each opinion. For example, the third opinion is attributed to the Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis, among many early scholars.
The fourth opinion is attributed to Abu Hanifa and his companions.
3
Identify the common proof for the majority
The passage states that one of the common proofs for the three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) is the hadith: 'The Prophet judged by the oath with the witness.' Recognize this as a key textual evidence.
The hadith itself is not quoted in full; it is referenced for further discussion.
4
Identify the Hanafi position
The fourth opinion, which rejects the oath with one witness absolutely, is the Hanafi position. Note that Abu Hanifa and his companions are explicitly named.
The Hanafi dalil is not given here but typically includes the Quranic verse on witnesses (2:282).
5
Understand the nature of the disagreement
Realize that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf arising from different usul methodologies, particularly regarding the acceptance of ahad hadith and the interpretation of the Quran.
Both sides have strong reasoning; the passage only states views, not full arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني / الاختلاف في المتن
روي هذا الثاني: يقضى به فيما سوى الحدود والقصاص، وهو قول الهادوية. الثالث: يقضى به في الأموال فقط، روي عن الخلفاء الأربعة، وأبي بن كعب، وابن عمر، وسعيد بن المسيب، والقاسم بن محمد، وأبي بكر بن عبد الرحمن، وخارجة بن زيد، وعبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عتبة، وسليمان بن يسار، والحسن، وشريح، وإياس بن معاوية، وعلي بن الحسين، ومحمد الباقر، وربيعة الرأي، وأبي الزناد، وابن أبي ليلى، وإسحاق، وأبي ثور، وأبي عبيد، وداود بن علي. وهو رواية عن عروة بن الزبير وعمر بن عبد العزيز، وروي عن ابن سيرين ويحيى بن يعمر، والزهري. وإليه ذهب المالكية والشافعية والحنابلة. وكانت إحدى الحجج المشتركة بين هذه المذاهب ثلاثتها حديث: «أن النبي قضى باليمين مع الشاهد». وسيأتي الكلام عنه. الرابع: روي أنه لا يقضى باليمين مع الشاهد في شيء مطلقاً. روي ذلك عن الشعبي، والنخعي، وعطاء، والثوري، والأوزاعي، وابن شبرمة، وإبراهيم، والحكم بن عتيبة. وهو رواية عن عروة بن الزبير والزهري وعمر بن عبد العزيز. وبه قال أبو حنيفة وأصحابه.
Chapter Two / Disagreement in the Text. It is reported this second (opinion): that judgment is given by it in other than hudud and qisas, and it is the saying of the Hadi school. The third: that judgment is given by it in financial matters only, reported from the four caliphs, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Ibn Umar, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, al-Qasim ibn Muhammad, Abu Bakr ibn Abd al-Rahman, Kharija ibn Zayd, Ubayd Allah ibn Abd Allah ibn Utba, Sulayman ibn Yasar, al-Hasan, Shurayh, Iyas ibn Mu'awiya, Ali ibn al-Husayn, Muhammad al-Baqir, Rabi'a al-Ra'y, Abu al-Zinad, Ibn Abi Layla, Ishaq, Abu Thawr, Abu Ubayd, and Dawud ibn Ali. And it is a transmission from Urwa ibn al-Zubayr and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and reported from Ibn Sirin, Yahya ibn Ya'mar, and al-Zuhri. And the Malikis, Shafi'is, and Hanbalis adopt it. And one of the common proofs among these three schools is the hadith: 'The Prophet judged by the oath with the witness.' And discussion about it will come. The fourth: it is reported that judgment is not given by the oath with the witness in anything at all. That is reported from al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, Ata, al-Thawri, al-Awza'i, Ibn Shubruma, Ibrahim, and al-Hakam ibn Utayba. And it is a transmission from Urwa ibn al-Zubayr, al-Zuhri, and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. And Abu Hanifa and his companions held this view.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the juristic disagreement (ikhtilaf) regarding the validity of judicial decision based on the 'oath with one witness' (al-yamin ma'a al-shahid). It lists four opinions: the first (not shown here), the second which accepts it except in hudud and qisas (attributed to the Hadi school, a Zaydi group), the third which accepts it only in financial matters (the view of the majority: Malikis, Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and many early scholars), and the fourth which rejects it entirely (the view of Abu Hanifa and his school). The common proof for the three schools is the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged by the oath with a witness. The Hanafi position, which rejects it absolutely, is based on their own interpretation and sources that will be discussed later. Note that the Hanafi madhhab strictly limits judicial decision to either full proof (bayyina) or admission (iqrar), and does not accept this hybrid procedure.

Key Terms
اليمين مع الشاهد (al-yamin ma'a al-shahid): A judicial procedure where a plaintiff's oath is accepted in addition to the testimony of one witness to establish a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school(s) accept(s) the oath with one witness in financial matters?
A) Only the Hanafi school
B) The Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali schools
C) Only the Hadi school
D) All schools including Hanafi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely rejects the oath with one witness entirely, highlighting how usul differences can lead to divergent fiqh rulings.
